From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Apr  1 00:52:21 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 00:52:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] IDE oddities
Message-ID: <200604010052.21504.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Anybody seen this sort of thing before?

  dd if=/dev/urandom of=/tmp/randfile bs=1024k count=5
  md5sum /tmp/randfile > /var/tmp/p1
  umount /tmp
  mount /tmp
  md5sum /tmp/randfile > /var/tmp/p2
  cat /var/tmp/p?

   e602832f95ef1f395c3dc98a4428b35a  /tmp/randfile
   0ba83cd02d0e182db2a551891a9ecaa9  /tmp/randfile

I can't quite figure out why some files change in subtle ways after 
remounting the drive.  At this point, I'm blaming the motherboard, 
power supply, or some other subtle issue.

I started in the installed OS with the drive on a promise pci card, got 
the same results with the on-board IDE, and again with a rescue CD, 
plus all of those scenarios with a different disk in the same box.  
Both drives work fine in a usb enclosure hung on a different machine.

What I can't figure out is why the other (root) drive gave me no trouble 
(though I didn't test that with the remounting, etc. and it's been up 
for the ~150 days since I installed it -- so maybe that drive didn't 
get unmounted enough times to suffer the bitrot?)

This is an old (200MHz NEC) box that's been through quite a lot.  I 
suppose it is time to hang it up and get something slightly younger and 
blonder from freegeek.

--Eric

I'm guessing that your cut'n'paste truncated the beginning of p1, as
it's too short (in addition to being "wrong")... In any case, if your
drive is as old as the CPU I wouldn't be surprised to see behaviour
like that at this point, presuming you don't have a really old/buggy
driver/filesystem combination.  Sounds like a good confirmation that
there is a hardware problem, though I'd expect to see if without the
unmount... though your file/pagecache is probably just assuming sanity
until it's flushed out.

I'd be curious of you started out with a file of known (non-binary)
data and diff'd it in the same scenario.. maybe it's some cosmic
message ;)

Or maybe it's too late... too bad I'm not up in Portland, I've got a
lot of overpowered hardware sitting in my basement.

/eli

From alex at daniloff.com  Sat Apr  1 08:11:57 2006
From: alex at daniloff.com (Alex Daniloff)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 08:11:57 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SCSI verses SATA RAID
In-Reply-To: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED716@cityemail.bit.city>
References: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED716@cityemail.bit.city>
Message-ID: <200604010811.57790.alex@daniloff.com>

This is a very good explanation of SCSI vs SATA

http://linuxmafia.com/faq/Hardware/sata.html

It explained precisely why I had so many problems with SATA raids.
In short, If your data is less important and valuable than your hardware, 
then go with SATA. Otherwise SCSI rules!
Cheap men always pay twice, first when they get a "bargain", second 
when they deal with it.

Enjoy

Alex

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Sat Apr  1 08:19:05 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Sat, 01 Apr 2006 08:19:05 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] CentOS /etc/yum.repos.d/CentOS-Base.repo broken, now fixed
Message-ID: <20060401161910.2251F8F14@tinman.seitzassoc.com>


Sometime in the past month or two I picked up a new
/etc/yum.repos.d/CentOS-Base.repo file via a yum update.  This file
was specifying port 81 in the mirrorlist URL.  Apparently they thought
this was a good idea, and then had second thoughts.  Anyway, there was
a window of time where an update might have picked up the port 81 version.
Today, if CentOS-Base.repo contains the port 81 version of the file, the
following error occurs:

$ yum list updates
Setting up repositories
Cannot find a valid baseurl for repo: update
Error: Cannot find a valid baseurl for repo: update

To fix the problem, just edit CentOS-Base.repo and remove ":81" from all
of the mirrorlist URLs.

galen

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Sat Apr  1 09:37:27 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 09:37:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] IDE oddities
In-Reply-To: <200604010124.25660.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200604010052.21504.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<f4468cae0604010102n6a7c0667pa2e8953c84857a05@mail.gmail.com>
	<200604010124.25660.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0604010937l256fe67aq56a364dc89e4bc3d@mail.gmail.com>

a sync() won't affect your read cache, which doesn't get deallocated
until (memory pressure|unmount|age) forces it out or something else
new in, so it's feasible that you had significant decay on-disk
immediately after writing but wouldn't "see" it until after that mount
cycling.  Try a smartctl -t long /dev/hd? on it, followed by a "-a"
and see if it reports any failure conditions, though I've seen a lot
of ATA drives that DON'T report critical stuff up through SMART (WD
mostly).

Let us know if you get any good ASCII art out of it ;)

/eli

I have a Dell 30" 2560x1600 attached to an eVGA 7800GT
(one dual-link DVI, one single-link DVI) and it is a joy to behold.

It is attached to a Supermicro dual-Opteron system running Debian unstable.
I am using the nVidia proprietary drivers vers 1.0.8178.
I had to tweak the xorg.conf a bit to include the 2560x1600 modes and to
explicitly set the DisplaySize (to get the font size to work better).

I also have a Dell 20" 1600x1200 next to it turned to portrait mode 
which makes it
the same "height" (physically, resolution, and DPI) as the 30".

I am happy with the 7800GT but, if buying the card today, I would go for 
the 7900GTX
so I had 2 dual-link DVI ports. (in case I win the lotto and want to 
have dual 30" :) )

Really obvious item, is /tmp tmpfs, in which case unmounting the
filesystem *does* make all the files disappear? Do you normally have a
different filesystem mounted there? (in which case umount would pull the
other filesystem out, and mount would bring in the normal FS)

> I can't quite figure out why some files change in subtle ways after 
> remounting the drive.  At this point, I'm blaming the motherboard, 
> power supply, or some other subtle issue.
>

> the same results with the on-board IDE, and again with a rescue CD, 
> plus all of those scenarios with a different disk in the same box.  
> Both drives work fine in a usb enclosure hung on a different machine.

Tried disabling DMA? Or using lower-speed DMA?

> suppose it is time to hang it up and get something slightly younger and 
> blonder from freegeek.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sat Apr  1 15:35:25 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 15:35:25 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Disto question
In-Reply-To: <442F03D3.3000401@verizon.net>
References: <442F03D3.3000401@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604011520300.6644@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, 1 Apr 2006, Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:

> The book I am reading didn't include Slackware in the mix of "most
> popular". I am under the impression that Slack is in the same style of
> "source" installation.

Bruce,

   No. Slackware comes as packages with a .tgz extension. One uses the
packagetools (installpkg, upgradepkg, removepkg) to manipulate them. There's
also swaret and slack<something> that can be used to upgrade constantly from
the current version (which will be the next stable release).

> The questions are...how do the two differ, which is better for a P3 933
> dual processor with 1.5 gig of memory, and which would be better if a
> slower machine wasn't a consideration?

   I've no idea which might be "better" for anything. I can tell you my
experiences, however.

   From 1997 (Red Hat 4.0 or .1, I forget which) through September 2003 (Red
Hat 7.3), that's all I used. Why? Because when I went to Powell's Tech Books
in early 1997, my choices were Red Hat, Slackware, and the other one with an
unpronounceable (and obviously forgettable) name and the clerk recommended
Red Hat just because they were selling more copies of that so it must have
been more popular.

   During this period I kept getting upgrade notices from the RH team, and
major upgrades were often a major PITA for me. Then, if I wanted to install
some productivity application (as distinct from a system or network
administration application), I frequently ended up in dependency hell.

   My research lead me to Slackware. It has less hand-holding than did Red
Hat, but it needs less, too. Once I made the transition from SysV-style init
scripts (used by RH and it's derivatives) to BSD-style init scripts, it was
a done deal. Other than security upgrades (which are quite infrequent), I
don't upgrade until Patrick Volkerding (who developed Slackware in 1994 and
still heads the team) send the next distribution to me (I'm on subscription so
it costs about $50 per year). It's rock stable (unless hardware goes), runs
everything I want or need, and just works in the background like a good OS
should.

   When I get an upgrade, it takes 15-30 minutes per machine (depending on
processor speed and the amount of memory). I telinit to single user, upgrade
the core packages, then tell it to upgrade everything I have on my system. A
new kernel is seamlessly installed, so I reboot back into runlevel 3 and off
I go again.

   Many folks use Slackware on servers and something else on the staff's
desktop. Patrick doesn't like Gnome, so he ships only KDE; he doesn't like
PostgreSQL, so he ships MySQL. He does ship Xfce. There is a great Web site,
http://www.linuxpackages.net, where I can get postgres, postfix, and almost
everything else I want or need as a Slack package.

   More importantly, when I build from a tarball of source code, after the
./configure and make steps, I run checkinstall and get a Slackware package
from that. I can install or upgrade that package and it's registered with all
the others in /var/log/packages.

   Everyone has a distribution that works for them. Mine is Slackware.

Rich

                                 PRESENTATION

                                   Inkscape

                                      by

                               Bryce Harrington
                               <bryce at osdl.org>
 
      Inkscape is an Open Source vector graphics editor, with capabilities 
      similar to Illustrator, Freehand, CorelDraw, or Xara X using the  
      W3C standard Scalable Vector Graphics  (SVG) file format. 
      Supported SVG features include shapes, paths, text, markers, 
      clones, alpha blending, transforms, gradients, patterns, and  
      grouping. Inkscape also supports Creative Commons meta-data, 
      node editing, layers, complex path operations, bitmap tracing, 
      text-on-path, flowed text, direct XML editing, and more. It imports 
      formats such as JPEG, PNG, TIFF, and others and exports PNG as 
      well as multiple vector-based formats.

      Inkscape's main goal is to create a powerful and convenient drawing 
      tool fully compliant with XML, SVG, and CSS standards. We also aim 
      to maintain a thriving user and developer community by using open, 
      community-oriented development process, and by making sure Inkcape 
      is easy to learn, to use, and to extend.

From aschlemm at comcast.net  Sat Apr  1 19:38:52 2006
From: aschlemm at comcast.net (Tony Schlemmer)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 19:38:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SCSI verses SATA RAID
In-Reply-To: <c29537840603311036u4332897mdea71f0f8004a154@mail.gmail.com>
References: <442D6249.6030707@acm.org> <1749-63308@sneakemail.com>
	<c29537840603311036u4332897mdea71f0f8004a154@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200604011938.52880.aschlemm@comcast.net>

On Friday 31 March 2006 10:36, Jason R. Martin wrote:
> Aren't a lot of the cheaper/low-end SATA "RAID" controllers actually
> software raid and/or RAID 0 and 1 only?
>
> Jason
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

At one company I worked at we wanted to have RAID-1 on several test systems 
that we had. The servers weren't super critical but it would be nice that 
they could stay up and no lose everything if a disk died. We looked at some 
of the cheaper SATA cards and they turned out to be some sort of funky 
hardware RAID just so we didn't bother with them.

7 months ago I put together a new workstation and got an LSI MegaRAID 150-4 
card which does real hardware RAID. I put a couple of Seagate NCQ SATA-150 
drives on the controller in a RAID-1 configuration. SuSE Linux detected the 
card out of the box and I didn't have to do anything really special to make 
it work with Linux. The only thing that must be done before the thing is 
useable is to go into the LSI setup program and create the logical disk 
volume.

I've been using nothing but SCSI for all of my systems since 1995 and so this 
last system I built to replace a dead workstation that didn't survive a move 
from Chicago is my first non-SCSI system in more than 10 years. I'm quite 
pleased with the system and Seagate SATA drives are much quieter than the 10K 
RPM  IBM Ultra-160 drives I had in my old workstation. I haven't done any 
speed comparisons or anything but the performance has been good enough for me 
so far. I didn't to any cost comparisons either but the OEM drive were $89.00 
a piece but I paid something like $235.00 for the LSI card so my solution 
wasn't exactly cheap since I spent over $400.00 just for the disk storage.

I'm still a hardware RAID SCSI bigot though if anyone wants me to build any 
kind of critical server systems for them though. :-D

Tony
 
-- 
Anthony Schlemmer
aschlemm at comcast.net


From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Apr  1 20:03:45 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 1 Apr 2006 20:03:45 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SCSI verses SATA RAID
In-Reply-To: <442D6249.6030707@acm.org>
References: <442D6249.6030707@acm.org>
Message-ID: <20060402040345.GM837@syrinxpc.com>

On Fri, Mar 31, 2006 at 09:09:29AM -0800, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> What are your thoughts on SCSI verses SATA RAID?

One is a proven, well-known technology with a lot of options out there,
the other gives you a much better dollars-to-gigabytes ratio and maybe a
better future.  (Though SCSI has a *lot* of momentum.)

If it's gonna be handed off to another sysadmin, use SCSI.

> Assume RAID cards not software RAID.

For SATA, 3Ware then.  Though Linux MD RAID is consistently competitive
with hardware RAID, with no worries about weird UIs, proprietary formats
or quicky finding a replacement RAID card.

> Cost is not the biggest issue though the cost savings of SATA mean 
> more/bigger drives.

Last server I built ended up using SCSI because it was built into the
mobo, and we weren't buying enough storage for the savings to cover the
cost of the SATA card.

> SCSI typically has a longer warranty ( 5 years ).

Actually, some SATA drives are consumer-quality short-warranty models,
and others are server-quality 5-year models.

Rich Shepard wrote:

> On Sat, 1 Apr 2006, Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> The book I am reading didn't include Slackware in the mix of "most
>> popular". I am under the impression that Slack is in the same style of
>> "source" installation.
>
>
> Bruce,
>
>   No. Slackware comes as packages with a .tgz extension. One uses the
> packagetools (installpkg, upgradepkg, removepkg) to manipulate them. 
> There's
> also swaret and slack<something> that can be used to upgrade 
> constantly from
> the current version (which will be the next stable release).
>
>> The questions are...how do the two differ, which is better for a P3 933
>> dual processor with 1.5 gig of memory, and which would be better if a
>> slower machine wasn't a consideration?
>
>
>   I've no idea which might be "better" for anything. I can tell you my
> experiences, however.
>
>   From 1997 (Red Hat 4.0 or .1, I forget which) through September 2003 
> (Red
> Hat 7.3), that's all I used. Why? Because when I went to Powell's Tech 
> Books
> in early 1997, my choices were Red Hat, Slackware, and the other one 
> with an
> unpronounceable (and obviously forgettable) name and the clerk 
> recommended
> Red Hat just because they were selling more copies of that so it must 
> have
> been more popular.
>
>   During this period I kept getting upgrade notices from the RH team, and
> major upgrades were often a major PITA for me. Then, if I wanted to 
> install
> some productivity application (as distinct from a system or network
> administration application), I frequently ended up in dependency hell.
>
>   My research lead me to Slackware. It has less hand-holding than did Red
> Hat, but it needs less, too. Once I made the transition from 
> SysV-style init
> scripts (used by RH and it's derivatives) to BSD-style init scripts, 
> it was
> a done deal. Other than security upgrades (which are quite infrequent), I
> don't upgrade until Patrick Volkerding (who developed Slackware in 
> 1994 and
> still heads the team) send the next distribution to me (I'm on 
> subscription so
> it costs about $50 per year). It's rock stable (unless hardware goes), 
> runs
> everything I want or need, and just works in the background like a 
> good OS
> should.
>
>   When I get an upgrade, it takes 15-30 minutes per machine (depending on
> processor speed and the amount of memory). I telinit to single user, 
> upgrade
> the core packages, then tell it to upgrade everything I have on my 
> system. A
> new kernel is seamlessly installed, so I reboot back into runlevel 3 
> and off
> I go again.
>
>   Many folks use Slackware on servers and something else on the staff's
> desktop. Patrick doesn't like Gnome, so he ships only KDE; he doesn't 
> like
> PostgreSQL, so he ships MySQL. He does ship Xfce. There is a great Web 
> site,
> http://www.linuxpackages.net, where I can get postgres, postfix, and 
> almost
> everything else I want or need as a Slack package.
>
>   More importantly, when I build from a tarball of source code, after the
> ./configure and make steps, I run checkinstall and get a Slackware 
> package
> from that. I can install or upgrade that package and it's registered 
> with all
> the others in /var/log/packages.
>
>   Everyone has a distribution that works for them. Mine is Slackware.
>
> Rich
>



From nixlists at writemoore.net  Sat Apr  1 22:00:30 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Sat, 01 Apr 2006 22:00:30 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] IPv6 resolved
Message-ID: <442F687E.2050808@writemoore.net>

A week or two ago, Elliot had suggested that the newer kernels licked 
the IPv6 bug that has plagued me for the past few months.  I installed 
Debian Etch on the new machine a few days ago and found, alas, that 
despite it using a 2.6.15 kernel, I still had all the same connection 
flakiness and still couldn't get anywhere without disabling IPv6.

Today I installed Arch Linux, which also uses a 2.6.15 kernel, and 
wonder of wonders, everything works.  No more having to ping servers 
before I can download packages, no more disabling of anything.  So it 
looks like that the problem is with Debian (and Ubuntu, and probably 
other Debian derivatives), although someone else reported similar issues 
with (IIRC) SuSE.  Maybe a networking guru could figure out what it is 
some distros are doing to mess up the packet routing.  Or maybe there 
are just too few people it affects for anyone to fix it.

So it looks like I *finally* have a free and clear connection, and it 
looks like I've found a new distro, which -- connection bonuses aside -- 
I'm really liking  so far.

dream." --S. Jackson

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sun Apr  2 09:36:12 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sun, 2 Apr 2006 09:36:12 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Linuxfest NW preliminary schedule
In-Reply-To: <20060402044059.GA25493@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20060402044059.GA25493@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604020915390.20553@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, 1 Apr 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> Given that we have so many people from the Portland are presenting, it
> might be could to schedule some practice sessions beforehand, that locals
> can watch. Does anyone want to help organize this, and find us a room? My
> living room is available, but I couldn't get more than 10 people in it. And
> the beer sucks...

Keith,

   Over the past quarter-century I have been subjected to very few well-done
presentations. Most are appallingly bad. For some reason, teaching how to
present an effective talk is not part of the educational or professional
training of lawywers, engineers, scientists, or ... computer geeks.

   In the mid-1970s Rosemary MacKay, Professor of Zoology at the University of
Toronto, gave a presention on how to give a presentation at an annual meeting
of the North American Benthological Society. It certainly made a strong
impression on me. Over the years I've read more on how to effectively present
a talk, and I've taken advantage of several free seminars here in the
Portland area that enhanced my understanding and presentation effectiveness
by addressing such topics as the colors used in the slides. Yes, it makes a
strong, sub-conscious impression on your audience by generating emotions that
either reinforce the message you're trying to convey or belying that message;
the latter leads to confusion in the listener's mind and you've lost your
audience.

   A couple of weeks ago I had to sit through a workshop on mixing zones (it
was one of those business things where I had to be seen in attendance). All
the speakers sucked big time; that's the most polite way to describe their
abuse of our presence.

   Unfortunately, too many of the PLUG meeting talks are of the same
ineffective type.

   I would be very happy to give a presentation/workshop/seminar on how to
give an effective presentation. This could help folks who not only preach to
the choir but need to make sales presentations (internally or externally),
speak to those who know less than they do about a particular topic, and be
better able to understand their own reactions to talks given by others.

   Three key factors with which an effective presentation must comply:

   1.)  Know your audience and what they expect to learn from you. Tell them
what you're going to say. Say it. Tell them what you said. Sit down.

   2.)  Assume that your audience can read and comprehend English. Do NOT
turn your back on the audience and read the slides to them. The slides should
be phrases; notes of the key points. Show few data; make it all readable to
someone over 40 years old sitting at the back of the audience. Clarity over
cute.

   3.)  If you want to excite your audience and get them emotionally stirred
up, use colors such as red and yellow. If you want to be taken seriously, use
colors such as green, blue, and grey. Use high contrast, preferably with a
dark background; black-on-white is much clearer than is white-on-black; red
on green gets you neutered with a rusty razor blade.

Rich

> On Sat, 1 Apr 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> 
> >Given that we have so many people from the Portland are presenting, it
> >might be could to schedule some practice sessions beforehand, that locals
> >can watch. Does anyone want to help organize this, and find us a room? My

On Sun, Apr 02, 2006 at 09:36:12AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   Over the past quarter-century I have been subjected to very few well-done
> presentations. Most are appallingly bad. For some reason, teaching how to
> present an effective talk is not part of the educational or professional
> training of lawywers, engineers, scientists, or ... computer geeks.
...

The best speaker among the locals I have seen is Randal Schwartz, when he
has time to carefully organize.  He gave a presentation to PLUG about how
to do it (about 2 years back, IIRC).  Besides the points Rich has already
made, an important point Randal made is to develop a realistic estimate
of how far you can take your audience in the time you have, and constrain
the scope of the presentation to match.

Some of the OSCON keynote speeches have been outstanding.  The best I've
seen recently is Dick Hardt's "Identity 2.0" talk:

    http://www.identity20.com/media/OSCON2005/ 

The best written exposition of how to do a presentation is in Guy Kawasaki's
"Art of the Start".  Guy's exemplar of masterful talks is Steve Jobs.
Though most of us do not have the resources of the Apple CEO, we can at
least do as well as Dick Hardt.

The process of learning this stuff from the masters is enjoyable.  Not
only will you learn something, you will have a good time while doing so;
a much better use of your relaxation time than watching TV.

>   A couple of weeks ago I had to sit through [deleted]
> the speakers sucked big time; that's the most polite way to describe their
> abuse of our presence.

Uh, Rich ... this forum is archived and searchable by the internet. 
While I can't think of a Google search that will lead those speakers
to your statement, it could be professionally costly if it did.  

Yes, there are a lot of bad presentations out there.  But it is a
learn-by-doing process.  Defensive people don't learn as well as open
people, so lessons about presentations (or anything else) should be 
provided with respect for the pupil.  Yes, they may start out as
idiots; but if they are still idiots at the end of the lesson, then
the teacher is an idiot, too.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

If you like Slack (or Gentoo) you may like Arch Linux even more.  It's i686
optimized -- boots and runs fast -- and has a "rolling release" packaging
system.  It also accommodates installation of unique packages through its
ABS system -- very nice.  I'd recommend one check out the wiki and forums
before taking the plunge.

I moved to Arch, a year ago, after trying Slack, Gentoo and others, and
after years of wrestling with RPM-hell.

My two cents ;-)

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Sun Apr  2 15:33:55 2006
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Sun, 2 Apr 2006 15:33:55 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] SCSI verses SATA RAID
In-Reply-To: <200604010811.57790.alex@daniloff.com>
References: <9AFECDC22C4D6942BDFFCBDFCDB27A97074ED716@cityemail.bit.city>
	<200604010811.57790.alex@daniloff.com>
Message-ID: <31504-74775@sneakemail.com>

On 4/1/06, Alex Daniloff wrote:
> This is a very good explanation of SCSI vs SATA
>
> http://linuxmafia.com/faq/Hardware/sata.html
>
> It explained precisely why I had so many problems with SATA raids.

It's a great discussion of SATA RAID controllers (and especially their
problems), but I wouldn't say it's a good discussion of SATA vs. SCSI.
 The references to SCSI in that document are pretty few and far
between.

That link includes a reference to http://spamaps.org/raidtests.php,
which describes some benchmarks on the Linux software RAID in recent
kernels and how it trounces hardware RAID for performance under
CPU-intensive RAID-5 tests.  This makes sense, since the fast general
purpose CPU in your box is likely to run those RAID-5 parity
calculations a whole lot faster than some cheap embedded CPU on a RAID
card, unless you really spend a lot on your RAID card.  However, I
generally avoid software RAID-5 since there are more things that can
go awry with it that will leave you without your data.  Mirroring is
great since in a worst-case scenario you can always just pull a drive
from the smoking ruin of your server, slap it into another one and
boot from it.  ;-)

  -- Steve

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Mon Apr  3 10:05:22 2006
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Mon, 3 Apr 2006 10:05:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Xen and the Art of OS Maintenance
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604030956450.25590@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604030956450.25590@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840604031005u260a5a1ep313dd0ce3d11a301@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/3/06, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Has anyone here used Xen to run Win98SE as a virtual guest on a linux host?
> If so, is there a mechanism to move applications and data from VMware to Xen?

Last I checked, Xen requires support from the OS you wish to run under
it, which is why only open-source OSs currently work with it.  The
researchers who developed it got some version of Windows (2000?)
working under it but were not able to release the changes they had to
make to Windows for licensing reasons.

VMware is able to emulate the entire hardware environment, hence the
ability to run almost anything under it.

Jason


From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Apr  3 10:07:12 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 3 Apr 2006 10:07:12 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Xen and the Art of OS Maintenance
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604030956450.25590@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604030956450.25590@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0604031003420.4153@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 3 Apr 2006, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  Has anyone here used Xen to run Win98SE as a virtual guest on a 
> linux host? If so, is there a mechanism to move applications and 
> data from VMware to Xen?

My understanding is that you need an Intel VT-aware processor (or, 
later this year, an AMD processor with "Pacifica" technology) to run 
Windows under Xen. The Xen Wiki has the best discussion I've run 
across of what processors will do this:

   http://wiki.xensource.com/xenwiki/IntelVT

That said, I haven't seen any documents that outline the process for 
getting Window 98 to run under Xen.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

> who developed it got some version of Windows (2000?) working under it but
> were not able to release the changes they had to make to Windows for
> licensing reasons.

Jason,

   I see.

> VMware is able to emulate the entire hardware environment, hence the
> ability to run almost anything under it.

   Yes. I've been using this since their pre-1.0 beta versions.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Apr  3 11:21:38 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 03 Apr 2006 11:21:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Thunderbird disk usage
Message-ID: <443167B2.4090300@comcast.net>

(I didn't ask the question here first, but since a number of you might 
be using Thunderbird, you might run into this someday. While this starts 
out with T-bird on Win98, it does continue to T-bird on SuSE 9.1.)

I use T-bird on this machine (SuSE) and on all the Win98 machines in the 
house (as well as three other machines that I "administer"). Sunday, 
after the change to daylight savings time, when my wife started up 
T-bird, it decided it had to rebuild all the summary files. After 
letting it do that, it was happy. She shut it down, and restarted it 
later and had to go through the same process. My own Win98 installation 
of T-bird did not exhibit this problem. In my case the program was 
running overnight, and I don't have too much e-mail on it, since I use 
this machine for most of it. And, naturally, this machine did not 
exhibit the problem, either. One other machine I take care of has 
exhibited the problem. It was shut down over the weekend, and restarted 
this morning. It doesn't have a lot of e-mail stored on it; my wife's 
machine does. I don't know if there's anything to that (volume of e-mail 
stored) or not.

I googled "Thunderbird rebuilding summary" and came up with this:

http://forums.mozillazine.org/viewtopic.php?t=397706

It recommended deleting all the .msf files and restarting T-bird. I 
suppose that if somehow the summary files were corrupted, this would fix 
that problem.

It also had a pointer to:

http://kb.mozillazine.org/Compacting_folders

The page starts with this explanation and warning:

----------------------------------------------------------------------
When you delete or move e-mails from a folder, Thunderbird and other 
e-mail programs really only "hide" them (i.e., mark them as ready for 
permanent deletion), and these hidden e-mails still actually remain in 
the folder. Even emptying the Trash does not permanently delete them. 
These hidden e-mails remain in the folder until it is compacted. If you 
don't compact folders your mail folders can grow very large and erratic 
program behavior occur, so it's a good idea to do it periodically.

Many new users never heard of compacting folders before and might 
confuse it with compressing files. It isn't some unique feature of 
Thunderbird. The main difference is that most other email clients by 
default automatically compact the folder when a certain amount of space 
is wasted. Thunderbird doesn't.
----------------------------------------------------------------------

I'm not a new user, but still, I was not aware that deleting mail from 
the trash folder did not delete the mail from T-birds data files.

I verified that this has the same effect on the Linux version of T-bird 
by running df before and after compacting the trash folder on this 
machine. I went from:

Filesystem           1K-blocks      Used Available Use% Mounted on
/dev/hda8              3670664   1940368   1730296  53% /home
to:
/dev/hda8              3670664   1844192   1826472  51% /home

There is a way to automate the process if one looks at

Edit>Preferences>Advanced>Manage Offline Settings...

There is a check box to "Compact folders when it will save over [100] 
KB" The [100] can be changed to whatever number of KB you want.

Hope this is useful to some of you.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Rich,

    You must be much more discriminating than I am.
I don't really care how polished a talk is as long
as I can understand the general idea of the presentation.
If I get one or two ideas out of a presentation 
(or a book for that matter) that are worth exploring on
my own, I consider the talk (or book) worth my time.
If I get one idea that I actually use in a major way,
I consider the presentation to be a Major Major Success.
By this this standand, 95% of the PLUG talks are worth my
time and a fair share of them are Major Major Successes.

    As a result, I am very grateful to our presenters for 
the high quality education they have been giving me since
1994.  They are worth every penny we have given them ...
and much much more.

On Sun, 2 Apr 2006, Rich Shepard wrote:

> On Sat, 1 Apr 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> 
> > Given that we have so many people from the Portland are presenting, it
> > might be could to schedule some practice sessions beforehand, that locals
> > can watch. Does anyone want to help organize this, and find us a room? My
> > living room is available, but I couldn't get more than 10 people in it. And
> > the beer sucks...
> 
> Keith,
> 
>    Over the past quarter-century I have been subjected to very few well-done
> presentations. Most are appallingly bad. For some reason, teaching how to
> present an effective talk is not part of the educational or professional
> training of lawywers, engineers, scientists, or ... computer geeks.
> 
>    In the mid-1970s Rosemary MacKay, Professor of Zoology at the University of
> Toronto, gave a presention on how to give a presentation at an annual meeting
> of the North American Benthological Society. It certainly made a strong
> impression on me. Over the years I've read more on how to effectively present
> a talk, and I've taken advantage of several free seminars here in the
> Portland area that enhanced my understanding and presentation effectiveness
> by addressing such topics as the colors used in the slides. Yes, it makes a
> strong, sub-conscious impression on your audience by generating emotions that
> either reinforce the message you're trying to convey or belying that message;
> the latter leads to confusion in the listener's mind and you've lost your
> audience.
> 
>    A couple of weeks ago I had to sit through a workshop on mixing zones (it
> was one of those business things where I had to be seen in attendance). All
> the speakers sucked big time; that's the most polite way to describe their
> abuse of our presence.
> 
>    Unfortunately, too many of the PLUG meeting talks are of the same
> ineffective type.
> 
>    I would be very happy to give a presentation/workshop/seminar on how to
> give an effective presentation. This could help folks who not only preach to
> the choir but need to make sales presentations (internally or externally),
> speak to those who know less than they do about a particular topic, and be
> better able to understand their own reactions to talks given by others.
> 
>    Three key factors with which an effective presentation must comply:
> 
>    1.)  Know your audience and what they expect to learn from you. Tell them
> what you're going to say. Say it. Tell them what you said. Sit down.
> 
>    2.)  Assume that your audience can read and comprehend English. Do NOT
> turn your back on the audience and read the slides to them. The slides should
> be phrases; notes of the key points. Show few data; make it all readable to
> someone over 40 years old sitting at the back of the audience. Clarity over
> cute.
> 
>    3.)  If you want to excite your audience and get them emotionally stirred
> up, use colors such as red and yellow. If you want to be taken seriously, use
> colors such as green, blue, and grey. Use high contrast, preferably with a
> dark background; black-on-white is much clearer than is white-on-black; red
> on green gets you neutered with a rusty razor blade.
> 
> Rich
>

From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Apr  3 13:06:53 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 03 Apr 2006 13:06:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Thunderbird disk usage
In-Reply-To: <443170E7.5080709@verizon.net>
References: <443167B2.4090300@comcast.net> <443170E7.5080709@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <4431805D.2060005@comcast.net>

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:

> I am using Thunderbird version 1.0.7 and I can't find the "Manage 
> Offline Settings" under preferences.

Edit>Preferences

brings up an Options window. The bottom icon on the left pane of that 
window is "Advanced". Clicking that puts a list of things in the right 
pane, one of which is labeled "Offline and Connection Settings". If you 
click the "+" sign next to that line you'll see "Manage offline 
settings." Over to the right of that is a button with the caption, 
"Offline Settings...". Clicking on that brings up another window with 
the title, "Offline & Disk Space". Note that this is the first time disk 
space has been mentioned. You'll find the check box to "Compact folders 
when it will save over [100]KB" near the bottom of this window.

Not exactly in the most obvious place. That's partly why I mentioned it.

HTH.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Mon Apr  3 13:42:05 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)

Subject: [PLUG] Disto question
In-Reply-To: <44318319.5080600@verizon.net>
References: <442F03D3.3000401@verizon.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0604011520300.6644@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<442F5FE9.3020404@verizon.net>	<53107f6e0604021312h5d4f6b52n4801b702176b31c8@mail.gmail.com>	<4430733F.10503@writemoore.net>	<53107f6e0604031306w67b2ca5bo6ef645cc8fa19cba@mail.gmail.com>
	<44318319.5080600@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <4431889D.30807@writemoore.net>

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> I went to grab a live CD so I could test drive it and found them lacking 
> in this offering...shucks!!
> 

See:  http://archie.dotsrc.org/

Archie is the liveCD version of Arch Linux, but it's a bit behind the 
times at the moment.  The latest version was released in November '05. 
It features an XFCE desktop.

Still, it should work.  Arch uses a rolling release system, so in effect 
the "releases" are just snapshots of the distro at a particular time.  I 
think just about everybody who uses Arch tracks the CURRENT repositories 
(and the adventurous also track the TESTING repositories), which means 
as long as you're installing software then your system is always 
up-to-date.  That also means that November '05, in Arch Linux terms, is 
ancient history.  It seems that a lot of the XFCE fans on the Arch 
message boards are using SVN builds of the next XFCE release, which are 
already packaged for Arch.  These folks live on the edge. :-)

BTW, the hardware detection tool (hwd is the name, I think) that Archie 
developers built for the live CD is also included in the main distro now 
and it's excellent.  It created an almost perfect xorg.conf file for me 
in about half-a-second.  The only thing wrong with it was a missing 
screen resolution, but that was easily fixed.  It's even better than 
Debian's 'dpkg-reconfigure xorg', which I thought was already a big 
improvement over 'xorg -configure'.

> Jon Scully wrote:
>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> I am using Thunderbird version 1.0.7 and I can't find the "Manage 
>> Offline Settings" under preferences.
>
>
> Edit>Preferences
>
> brings up an Options window. The bottom icon on the left pane of that 
> window is "Advanced". Clicking that puts a list of things in the right 
> pane, one of which is labeled "Offline and Connection Settings". If 
> you click the "+" sign next to that line you'll see "Manage offline 
> settings." Over to the right of that is a button with the caption, 
> "Offline Settings...". Clicking on that brings up another window with 
> the title, "Offline & Disk Space". Note that this is the first time 
> disk space has been mentioned. You'll find the check box to "Compact 
> folders when it will save over [100]KB" near the bottom of this window.
>
> Not exactly in the most obvious place. That's partly why I mentioned it.
>
> HTH.
>
I am using the Debian version of Thunderbird version 1.0.7 (20051017). I 
found everthing there as described.

> > presentations. Most are appallingly bad. For some reason, teaching how to
> > present an effective talk is not part of the educational or professional
> > training of lawywers, engineers, scientists, or ... computer geeks.
> > 
> >    In the mid-1970s Rosemary MacKay, Professor of Zoology at the University of
> > Toronto, gave a presention on how to give a presentation at an annual meeting
> > of the North American Benthological Society. It certainly made a strong
> > impression on me. Over the years I've read more on how to effectively present
> > a talk, and I've taken advantage of several free seminars here in the
> > Portland area that enhanced my understanding and presentation effectiveness
> > by addressing such topics as the colors used in the slides. Yes, it makes a
> > strong, sub-conscious impression on your audience by generating emotions that
> > either reinforce the message you're trying to convey or belying that message;
> > the latter leads to confusion in the listener's mind and you've lost your
> > audience.
> > 
> >    A couple of weeks ago I had to sit through a workshop on mixing zones (it
> > was one of those business things where I had to be seen in attendance). All
> > the speakers sucked big time; that's the most polite way to describe their
> > abuse of our presence.
> > 
> >    Unfortunately, too many of the PLUG meeting talks are of the same
> > ineffective type.
> > 
> >    I would be very happy to give a presentation/workshop/seminar on how to
> > give an effective presentation. This could help folks who not only preach to
> > the choir but need to make sales presentations (internally or externally),
> > speak to those who know less than they do about a particular topic, and be
> > better able to understand their own reactions to talks given by others.
> > 
> >    Three key factors with which an effective presentation must comply:
> > 
> >    1.)  Know your audience and what they expect to learn from you. Tell them
> > what you're going to say. Say it. Tell them what you said. Sit down.
> > 
> >    2.)  Assume that your audience can read and comprehend English. Do NOT
> > turn your back on the audience and read the slides to them. The slides should
> > be phrases; notes of the key points. Show few data; make it all readable to
> > someone over 40 years old sitting at the back of the audience. Clarity over
> > cute.
> > 
> >    3.)  If you want to excite your audience and get them emotionally stirred
> > up, use colors such as red and yellow. If you want to be taken seriously, use
> > colors such as green, blue, and grey. Use high contrast, preferably with a
> > dark background; black-on-white is much clearer than is white-on-black; red
> > on green gets you neutered with a rusty razor blade.
> > 
> > Rich
> >

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> Okay...I have downloaded Slax 5.1 and Mepis.  Neither one detects my 
> mouse (a Logitech trackball) and leaves me stranded as I am not sharp 
> enough to open a terminal window or to know what file needs to be 
> edited.  The computer is a K6II 350.  Should I go looking for something 
> special this old girl?


There's a good, relatively recent article looking at distros for older 
hardware at Linux.com: http://www.linux.com/article.pl?sid=06/02/13/1854251

The file you'd need to edit to fix your mouse is /etc/X11/xorg.conf 
(could be /etc/X11/XF86config, but I think most distros have moved to 
Xorg by now).  There should be two device sections, one for your 
keyboard and one for the mouse.  What's wrong might or might not be 
obvious, depending on what it says, but it's a start.

Michael M. wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> I went to grab a live CD so I could test drive it and found them

>
> See:  http://archie.dotsrc.org/
>
> Archie is the liveCD version of Arch Linux, but it's a bit behind the 
> times at the moment.  The latest version was released in November '05. 
> It features an XFCE desktop.
>
> Still, it should work.  Arch uses a rolling release system, so in 
> effect the "releases" are just snapshots of the distro at a particular 
> time.  I think just about everybody who uses Arch tracks the CURRENT 
> repositories (and the adventurous also track the TESTING 
> repositories), which means as long as you're installing software then 
> your system is always up-to-date.  That also means that November '05, 
> in Arch Linux terms, is ancient history.  It seems that a lot of the 
> XFCE fans on the Arch message boards are using SVN builds of the next 
> XFCE release, which are already packaged for Arch.  These folks live 
> on the edge. :-)
>
> BTW, the hardware detection tool (hwd is the name, I think) that 
> Archie developers built for the live CD is also included in the main 
> distro now and it's excellent.  It created an almost perfect xorg.conf 
> file for me in about half-a-second.  The only thing wrong with it was 
> a missing screen resolution, but that was easily fixed.  It's even 
> better than Debian's 'dpkg-reconfigure xorg', which I thought was 
> already a big improvement over 'xorg -configure'.
>

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Tue Apr  4 00:44:13 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Tue, 04 Apr 2006 00:44:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] befuddled again
Message-ID: <443223CD.5030005@writemoore.net>

A brief recap:  since I first installed Linux on my iMac three months or 
so ago, I've had connection issues related to IPv6.  This was under both 
Debian and Ubuntu.  The "fix" involved editing /etc/modules.d/aliases to 
disable IPv6 support; however, it wasn't a perfect fix.  Although that 
did enable me to browse the web, I still had problems downloading from 
the apt repositories and doing things like using Bittorrent.  Basically, 
I always had to ping the servers I wanted to connnect to, or it was no go.

Last week I got a new machine with Ubuntu pre-installed.  Same problem. 
  I installed Debian Etch to see if a newer kernel fixed the problem. 
Elliot had read that it was fixed recently.  Unfortunately, I had the 
same result.

Keep in mind that all this time I had no troubles at all with OS X on my 
iMac.

Saturday I installed Arch Linux on the new machine, and happily it 
worked properly from the get-go.  After playing around with it, I 
decided to re-structure my system to try out a few things.  I wiped 
everything and started by reinstalling Ubuntu; I was then going to 
reinstall Arch, and forget Debian.  Much to my surprise, during the 
install Ubuntu managed to connect to the repositories.  Neither it nor 
Debian have done that before, I've always had to go back post-install 
and edit the apt sources.list, ping the servers, then connect.  When the 
install finished, I booted into Ubuntu and discovered that everything 
just worked -- no disabling IPv6 necessary, no pinging necessary. 
Firefox worked fine instead of timing out like it always had.

So curiosity got the better of me and I reinstalled Debian after all, 
the exact same disc I'd used last week before installing Arch Linux.  It 
also worked immediately, and is still working.  IPv6 is enabled (that 
is, ifconfig shows an IPv6 address) and it looks like all the problems 
that have plagued me for the past three months are over.

The only thing that changed between last week and this week is that I 
installed a distro that didn't have these problems.  How could that 
possibly fix the distros that did?  It's not as if I didn't have an OS 
on hand that worked -- OS X worked fine even with Debian or Ubuntu 
sharing the same machine.  I don't see how it could have been a hardware 
issue, but could there be some setting in the modem that was screwing up 
Debian and Ubuntu, but not OS X?  And somehow Arch Linux fixed it?

Any enlightenment would be welcome.  I just don't understand this.

-- 
"No live organism can continue for long to exist sanely under conditions 
of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to 
dream." --S. Jackson

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Apr  4 06:15:02 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 4 Apr 2006 06:15:02 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Disto question
In-Reply-To: <4432000D.7020607@verizon.net>

	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0604011520300.6644@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

	<53107f6e0604031306w67b2ca5bo6ef645cc8fa19cba@mail.gmail.com>
	<44318319.5080600@verizon.net> <4431889D.30807@writemoore.net>
	<4432000D.7020607@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604040606310.14151@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 3 Apr 2006, Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:

> Okay...I have downloaded Slax 5.1 and Mepis.  Neither one detects my mouse
> (a Logitech trackball) and leaves me stranded as I am not sharp enough to
> open a terminal window or to know what file needs to be edited. The
> computer is a K6II 350. Should I go looking for something special this old
> girl?

Bruce,

   Several weeks ago my old Kensington Orbit trackball broke, and I replaced
it with a Logitech. Unplugged one; plugged in the other. Both are seen as
PS/2 pointy things.

   Is your problem on the console or in the X Window System? If the former, is
/usr/sbin/gpm running? In the latter, what have you in /etc/X11/xorg.conf?

   On my system, the latter contains:

# Identifier and driver

     Identifier  "Kensington Orbit"
     Driver      "mouse"

   (Notice I did not change the identifier when I changed the hardware.)

# The mouse device.  The device is normally set to /dev/mouse,
# which is usually a symbolic link to the real device.

#    Option "Device"      "/dev/mouse"
    Option "Device"      "/dev/psaux"
#   Option "Device"      "/dev/ttyS0"
#   Option "Device"      "/dev/ttyS1"

   (Because the trackball is plugged into the PS/2 socket on the system
board.)

HTH,

Rich

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> I did some more looking and found under the File menu, just above empty 
> trash, there is a "compact folders".
> 
> Does this count?

That's the recommended thing to do that will actually free up disk space 
and really delete deleted e-mail. You can also compact a specific folder 
by right clicking on it and choosing "Compact this folder" from the 
context menu.

The automation feature I found pops up a dialog that offers to compact 
the folders when T-bird decides that doing so will save 100 KB or more 
of space. The more important thing is that compaction be done from time 
to time, so the automation feature is not critical as long as you do it 
yourself.

Regards,

Dick Steffens

SQL-Ledger is great, though I don't think I would recommend it as a Quicken 
replacement, as it's more of a business accounting applicatoin. Moneydance is 
awesome for personal finances, http://www.moneydance.com/. There are other 
Linux personal finance applications, but none of them have the polish, 
feature set, or usability of Moneydance.

http://www.appgen.com/aptus/my_books_professional_linux2.htm
Appgen MyBooksPro is a great Quickbooks replacement, also great for business 
finances. 

Crossover Office runs Quicken well on Linux, 
http://www.codeweavers.com/products/cxoffice/, so you can keep Quicken until 
you're ready to move to something else. A big problem with migrating away 
from Quicken is exporting your data. Intuit makes this as unfriendly and 
difficult as possible, they even dropped QIF support. Which is another reason 
to dump their sorry products.

Rich,

On Mon, 2006-04-03 at 10:53 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Mon, 3 Apr 2006, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> 
> > My understanding is that you need an Intel VT-aware processor (or, later
> > this year, an AMD processor with "Pacifica" technology) to run Windows
> > under Xen. The Xen Wiki has the best discussion I've run across of what
> > processors will do this:
> 
>    Thanks, Paul. I saw a brief article today on a company that's open sourcing
> its Xes-based tool. That's what got me interested.
> 
> > That said, I haven't seen any documents that outline the process for getting 
> > Window 98 to run under Xen.
> 
>    OK. I'll stay with VMware. Apparently it's going to cost me money
> regardless of solution selected.
> 
I've gotten Win98 to run under QEMU, it was slow, but usable on a 2.8Gig
P4.  There is a kernel patch that will supposedly increase performance 4
fold, but I haven't had time to try it.  (I was able to get a base
install of 2K running under QEMU, but it lost access to a critical file
after applying SP4, and would not boot any more.)  With win98, you
should be able to copy the file system to a QEMU virtual disk, boot to
safe mode, wipe the hardware, then boot to normal mode.  AFAIK, VMware
virtual disks are not compatible with QEMU and QEMU disks are not
compatible with VMware.  Good thing they both have network support.

Good Luck,
-- 
Derek Loree <drl at drloree.com>

Christian wrote:
> Michael,
> 
> Did you break the partitions and rebuild them, or did you just leave

> partitions and rebuilt them, then installed Debian... I had issues, many 
> issues. So, I reinstalled Debian, using a different disk, and net 
> installing then I had all the same ol crap. Each time I installed 
> Debian, it was a bear. This was the worst experience that I have ever 
> had installing Debian. (I have installed it about 15 times.) Thanks be 
> to God that #oklinux is on freenode, or else I would have taken a year 
> to get it to work! If you need help, go there, and ask Cybodog for some.
> 
> christian

By "break" I presume you mean delete the existing partitions and create 
new ones.  Another new term for me! :-)

Actually, this time I did delete the existing partitions.  The reason 
for re-doing the set-up was because I came up with a scheme that (I 
hope) will last me for awhile without too much reshuffling.  I wanted 
new partitions sizes, and I decided it was easiest just to delete all 
them and start from scratch.  On the iMac, I usually did a new install 
into existing partitions, since I was only ever sharing OS X with one 
Linux OS at a time.  Apple does not provide an easy means for resizing 
OS X, so it's more difficult to change things around too much on a Mac 
than it is on a PC.  In any case, I hadn't thought that there might be 
something persisting on the drive between installs.  Until recently, I 
thought a re-install automatically erased all the existing data.

Debian was installed on the new machine last week on newly created 
partitions that had been unpartitioned empty space on the hard drive, 
and Debian still exhibited the same old problems.  Arch Linux, too, was 
installed over formerly empty space.  When I wiped all the existing 
partitions yesterday, I created new partitions using the same filetype, 
so maybe there was still something left.  But since the pre-installed 
Ubuntu was at the beginning of the drive and Arch was farther along, I 
don't see how an Ubuntu reinstall (also at the beginning of the drive) 
could've picked up files from a wiped Arch install about 45GBs away.

One thing I forgot about:  before I installed Arch Linux, I booted into 
FreeBSIE, the FreeBSD live CD.  It came up fine except that it failed to 
start my NIC, so I had no internet connection.  I didn't really do too 
much investigating -- I don't know if it failed to detect it, or just 
didn't load the right module.  (Is there a meaningful difference between 
those?)  I was mostly just looking at how the filesystem conventions 
differed.  So I don't think that would've done the trick, but this whole 
thing puzzles me so who knows?

What kind of issues did you have installing Debian?  Apart from my 
connection woes, I really haven't had any problems with either Sarge or 
Etch installs.  These last two reinstalls were using the Etch beta2 
installer.

I find many of these large integer names like this get resolved.
These are names without a .com or .org or .de or .whatever
Are these legal names?
Are they in some reserved name space?
Reserved for the Chinese Phishing Industry perhaps?

From aaron at madebyai.com  Tue Apr  4 09:32:02 2006
From: aaron at madebyai.com (Aaron Ten Clay)
Date: Tue, 4 Apr 2006 09:32:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Domain Naming Question: Why does the name 3546435852
	resolve?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0604040925010.3593@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0604040925010.3593@PPC.DavidMandel.com>
Message-ID: <200604040932.09905.aaron@madebyai.com>

On Tue April 4 2006 09:39, David Mandel wrote:
> 
>     Someone sent me a phishing note that didn't get killed by my spam
> filter, so I got distracted and looked at it.  Surprisingly, it trys
> to trick one into going to:
> 
>          http://3546435852/SomeLongPathName which fishes paypal
> 
> I didn't think "3546435852" was a valid name.
> However, my DNS seems to resolve it to 211.98.81.12 which is an
> address in China (Big Surprise).
> 

That's a decimal encoded IP address. The same as typing http://211.98.81.12/SomeLongPathName into your browser. There's no DNS resolution happening at all.

A quick wikipedia search doesn't reveal a terribly useful page describing the concept, but basically take the 32 bit binary address and convert that to decimal to get a decimal encoding. Traditionally we take 8 bits at a time and convert them to decimal, separated by dots. Just two different ways to represent the same thing.

-Aaron

Michael M. wrote:
> The only thing that changed between last week and this week is that I
> installed a distro that didn't have these problems.

Rephrase that to "The only thing I'm aware of ..."
> How could that possibly fix the distros that did?

It didn't.  There was something in your initial Ubuntu or Debian install that you
didn't notice or are forgetting.

This is one of the features that I love about working with deterministic systems
like computers.  They are consistant, they don't lie (not even to themselves) and
with enough digging you can get to the bottom of any problem.

Soduko puzzles share the same traits.  In the process you either come to the
conclusion/answer or you backtrack to a known good point and redo until you've got
it right.

While you have no way of recovering your old Ubuntu/Debian install to dissect it. 
But you know this:  the installs are different, even if you don't recall anything
different.  The actions of the computer show this.

-- 
   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity
        http://www.patch.com/words/

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> I did some more looking and found under the File menu, just above 
>> empty trash, there is a "compact folders".
>>
>> Does this count?
>
>
> That's the recommended thing to do that will actually free up disk 
> space and really delete deleted e-mail. You can also compact a 
> specific folder by right clicking on it and choosing "Compact this 
> folder" from the context menu.
>
> The automation feature I found pops up a dialog that offers to compact 
> the folders when T-bird decides that doing so will save 100 KB or more 
> of space. The more important thing is that compaction be done from 
> time to time, so the automation feature is not critical as long as you 
> do it yourself.
>

load installer
walk through installation choosing
partitioning for workstation
install desktop environment
choose correct time, region settings
everything is good.
reboot system

brings up tty1 login (command) prompt
login
system stays at command prompt (no command given by system)
startx
x makes the attempt and quits
hmmm no desktop environment?
<install gdm>
even though I had chosen desktop environment (like the previous times) 
there is no desktop environment
ok, so I think hmm... maybe there is something wrong with xorg settings 
check them they ok, but need change mouse settings to psaux
restart
same login prompt
.... same thing
no desktop environment
so from here I basically install the whole desktop environment and 
remove xdm which was installed (but probably broken)

to make a long drawn out story short...

>> mouse

>> enough to
>> open a terminal window or to know what file needs to be edited. The
>> computer is a K6II 350. Should I go looking for something special 
>> this old
>> girl?
>
>
> Bruce,
>
>   Several weeks ago my old Kensington Orbit trackball broke, and I 
> replaced
> it with a Logitech. Unplugged one; plugged in the other. Both are seen as
> PS/2 pointy things.
>
>   Is your problem on the console or in the X Window System? If the 
> former, is
> /usr/sbin/gpm running? In the latter, what have you in 
> /etc/X11/xorg.conf?
>
>   On my system, the latter contains:
>
> # Identifier and driver
>
>     Identifier  "Kensington Orbit"
>     Driver      "mouse"
>
>   (Notice I did not change the identifier when I changed the hardware.)
>
> # The mouse device.  The device is normally set to /dev/mouse,
> # which is usually a symbolic link to the real device.
>
> #    Option "Device"      "/dev/mouse"
>    Option "Device"      "/dev/psaux"
> #   Option "Device"      "/dev/ttyS0"
> #   Option "Device"      "/dev/ttyS1"
>
>   (Because the trackball is plugged into the PS/2 socket on the system
> board.)
>
> HTH,
>
> Rich

>right here in PLUG-land. 
>
>So tell me more about the dual Opteron motherboard and stuff. I 
>was thinking of going with a single dual-core AMD-64, probably a 
>couple steps below cutting edge, as CPU speed is probably not 
>that big a deal to me. At the same time, I want a pretty fast hard 
>disk. Plus I want the rest of the goodies -- a couple DVD+_RW 
>drives, and maybe even a TV tuner. Standard 10/100 ethernet is all 
>I need and otherrwise nothing special. Do you recommend the 
>Suptermicro motherboard, and if so, which one?
>
>Also, which distro are you using, and is it the 64-bit version?
>
>And do you have any other words of advice?
>  
>
System under discussion:

Supermicro AW4020C composed of Supermicro H8DCE dual Opteron motherboard 
and
Supermicro 645 rack/tower case with temp controlled case/mb/PS fans and 
8-SATA hot-swap bays.
Areca 1220 PCIe 8-port SATA HW RAID controller
eVGA 7800 GT KO video card
Pioneer 110D DVD DL/RW
Hauppgauge PVR550 dual TV tuner
Creative Audigy 2Z (includes firewire port "missing" from MB)

Getting this system up and running has been a real PITA.  Mostly due to 
bad vendor experience exacerbated by bad or misleading 
compatibility/driver information.

I purchased the Supermicro AW4020C barebones system (which contains 
Supermicro H8DCE motherboard), (2) 246 Opterons, and (4) 1G OCZ ECC 
PC3200 RAM  from Monarch Computers.  I purchased as "parts" because 
Monarch could not build the system with the parts I wanted.  I purchased 
the rest of the components from TigerDirect, Newegg, Directron, and 
QuietPC.  I prefer to keep the vendor count down but, in this case, no 
one could provide all the components.  For example, I wanted the eVGA 
7800 GT (to get dual-link DVI) but only TigerDirect had them in stock.  
I purchased my fanless CPU coolers from QuietPC because they were, and 
always have been, very helpful in determining what would or would not 
work in the system.

I now, personally, consider Monarch Computer my "vendor of last 
resort".  There service and support was so bad I would only purchase 
something from them if I could not get it ANY PLACE else.  They are 
supposedly OEM for the Supermicro products but were totally unable to 
provide support.  I had a problem which turned out to be a bad 
motherboard; I sent the system back; after waiting the alloted time for 
it's return it took me days just to get someone to answer the phone 
where upon they just shipped the system back without fixing it (not sure 
they even looked at it).

Once I contacted Supermicro and pleaded for direct support (which they 
readily granted) things were diagnosed and fixed very quickly.  They 
cross-shipped a new motherboard FedEx and things were moving on.

Which got things working enough to discover the (5) Samsung 2504C 250G 
SATA drives I purchased have a bug in their implementation of SATA-II 
features and can not be detected by the Areca RAID.  This is a 
known/fixed problem but requires drives with newer/upgraded firmware.  
It took me weeks, and several calls to the Samsung national sales 
manager to receive firware upgrade software which immediately rendered 
the drive dead.  More calls to the national sales manager and I received 
(5) new drives ALL with the outdated firware.  I gave up on the Samsung 
drives.

The Seagate 7200.9 500G drives now in the machine work just great.

Unfortunately, they only work attached directly to the mother board 
nForce SATA ports.  I could install onto the Areca based RAID array 
using SuSE 10.0 or Ubuntu 5.10 BUT both exhibited such instability (I 
think related to, then current, AMD64/nvidia problems) that I could not 
use the system.  I switched to Debian (which I use on a couple other 
systems) and now have a very stable system.  Unfortunately, the Areca 
driver is not included in the Debian kernels (not even the newest 
ones).  There are sarge install variants available which include the 
Areca driver but as soon as you upgrade your kernel you loose the 
Areca.  Bottom line -- Areca and Debian only work if you are into 
building you own kernel/drivers.

TigerDirect, Newegg, Directron, Dell, and especially QuietPC were all 
very, very good to deal with.

Now, having listened to my home movie, let me say my plans for the next 
system.

1) I will not buy another RAID card unless I KNOW it is supported in the 
standard kernel.  It is just too much ongoing pain and hassle if you're 
just trying to maintain/use the machine.  Every time I have been swayed 
to buy the newer/better/shinier SCSI, RAID, whatever it has not been 
cost-effective to get the thing working and maintain it. The safest is 
3ware which is well supported in most distributions and has been for 
some time. 

2) I will probably buy the Tyan motherboard next time (eg. 2895).  The 
Supermicro people have been very good when I have a bad component but 
there are very few forums or other resources available to get help.  
Supermicro's Opteron products are kind of their quiet secret and just 
not nearly as widely/publically supported as the Tyan boards are.

3) I would highly recommend the Hauppgauge PVR 150/250/350/550 cards.  
Not only are they good products but they seem to be the best supported 
(eg. in MythTV project, etc.).

4) For desktop/workstation system I would probably, at this point, go 
with a AMD64 X2 solution using a DFI, MSI, or Tyan motherboard.  The 
system above needed to include a large disk array and will eventually 
migrate into primarily server role so I went with the more expensive 
"server" hardware.  Probably overkill the desktop/workstation use.

5) Get the Apple or Dell 30"; it is "sell a body part" worth it (at 
least for the development / graphics work I do).  At least make sure you 
get a dual-link DVI card so you can support a 30" (2560x1600) or 24" 
(1920x1200) when you finally get paid for all those blood donations :)

6) If you install AMD64 version (esp of Debian) you will need to set up 
a 32-bit chroot environment;  it turned out to be a lot simpler to set 
up than it appeared at first blush and works quite well.

Hope that is not too longwinded...

R.Parr, RHCE, Temporal Arts
Portland, OR  U.S.A.

>Of course.  This is simple enough.

>On Tue, 4 Apr 2006, Aaron Ten Clay wrote:
>
> > On Tue April 4 2006 09:39, David Mandel wrote:
> > >
> > >     Someone sent me a phishing note that didn't get killed by my spam
> > > filter, so I got distracted and looked at it.  Surprisingly, it trys
> > > to trick one into going to:
> > >
> > >          http://3546435852/SomeLongPathName which fishes paypal
> > >
> > > I didn't think "3546435852" was a valid name.
> > > However, my DNS seems to resolve it to 211.98.81.12 which is an
> > > address in China (Big Surprise).
> > >
> >
> > That's a decimal encoded IP address. The same as typing 
> http://211.98.81.12/SomeLongPathName into your browser. There's no DNS 
> resolution happening at all.
> >
> > A quick wikipedia search doesn't reveal a terribly useful page 
> describing the concept, but basically take the 32 bit binary address and 
> convert that to decimal to get a decimal encoding. Traditionally we take 
> 8 bits at a time and convert them to decimal, separated by dots. Just two 
> different ways to represent the same thing.
> >
> > -Aaron
> >

From ladyfractal at yahoo.com  Tue Apr  4 11:02:16 2006
From: ladyfractal at yahoo.com (Adrienne J Davis)

Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu Dapper Drake Officially Delayed
In-Reply-To: <4429876A.22970.B6A1D5EF@localhost>

	<4429876A.22970.B6A1D5EF@localhost>
Message-ID: <200604041102.27512.ladyfractal@yahoo.com>

John:

I certainly understand what you are saying and I would come down closer to your position, however I wouldn't blame Linux, I would blame the community.  Although, at some level, this is a very open community (and I speak here of the Linux community in the most generic sense not PLUG) educating people is considered almost an afterthought.  It is almost as if the general consensus is that of course avergage end-user Mary is going to want to spend Saturday night hacking config files.  Who wouldn't want to?  And therein lies part of the problem.  I will say that over the last eight or nine years, the Linux/open-source community has moved away from the kind of obvious elitism (and there is no other word for it than that, sorry) that caused folks to disdain usability concerns (although never explicitly stated, the feeling I got was that the learning curve kept the 'mundanes' away) as irrelevant.  Anyone who really wanted to learn how to network with Samba would hack smb.conf end-of-story.  There is a truth as far as that goes, however, if you are trying to network your house and you just can't make Samba work, how likely are you to recommend Linux to your neighbor?  If not by word of mouth, how does the Linux meme spread?  I think that the open-source community as a whole and the Linux community specifically needs to do a much better job at educating people and remembering 'beginner's mind'.  We also need to take the issue of end-user experience as important and not an inconvenient sidenote.
Cheers
Aj

On Tue, 2006-04-04 at 10:59 -0700, Mike Neal wrote:
> Hi,
> 
> Is there a way to use the 32 bit binary value in the address?

I don't think so.  Most IP address parsers (I suspect) tend to be
implemented with something like scanf(3), which handles decimal, octal
and hexadecimal representation of integers.  It is, however, possible to
use the hexadecimal representation; I've been known to be obnoxious and
enter netmasks in hex--typically 0xffffff00.

I run Win98 under Win4Lin 4, in order to run one CAD app.  The CAD app
sometimes bluescreens the Win98 and Win4Lin, but nothing closing the
window can't handle.  Since the earlier versions of Win4Lin translated
to the native Linux file system,  this leaves less file corruption than
a native Windows crash.  Newer versions of Win4Lin unfortunately work
like VMWare, with a large binary disk file that pretends it is the
Windoze file system.  That may crash more deeply.  This is so people
can run XP and NTFS, I suppose.

Are there any websites out there that assist with on-the-spot
Linux-to-Projector interfacing?   If not, perhaps there is a
business opportunity here.

I was imagining a site with a webform where you enter your laptop
type, distro or version of X, and the desired resolution, and it
emits a commented Xconfig file.  The deluxe version of the site
helps you locate and upload your current Xconfig file, and then
sends it back with the necessary changes.  The superdeluxe version
allows institutions to upload the list of projectors they have in
various rooms, so an employee or visitor can enter something like
"XYZ company, conference room B-3" and get setup info in advance
(without revealing theft-friendly info).  

That would be a medium-sized task to do all the CGI programming
for, but one motivated person could probably do it in a few
months.  It could get paid for by bonus features and advertising
- it would be used by folks with more money than most.  And no,
it would be evil to put the advertising in the user's presentation,
don't even think that :-/

Of course you could extend the site to handle Windows and
Macintosh, but hardly anyone uses those OS's any more ...

Somebody please steal the idea and roll it out, so I have
something to use when I try to get my laptop working with some
ancient 640x480 P.O.S. projector, or some 2048x1536 laptop with
my 1024x768 projector, 5 minutes before an important presentation.

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Apr  4 17:49:10 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 4 Apr 2006 17:49:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux-to-projector website?
In-Reply-To: <4432FE88.6000505@rocksolidservice.com>
References: <20060404213833.GC5421@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<4432FE88.6000505@rocksolidservice.com>
Message-ID: <20060405004910.GA3023@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, Apr 04, 2006 at 04:17:28PM -0700, Christian wrote:
> Keith,
> 
> I thought that projectors were 'dumb' machines that merely receive a 
> signal and then convert it to light on a screen... I have not had any 
> issues with this... but admittedly, I am only working with one 
> projector. I have an old piece o junk sony viao that sends the signal 
> quite well.

All computer projectors are displays, and all modern ones incorporate
some kind of solid state pixel array, with a fixed sized X by Y size.
The signal out of a laptop HD15 video port makes assumptions about
the X by Y size of the pixel array, typically assuming it matches the
laptop display.  By changing the X configuration on the laptop, you
can change the X by Y size of the signal.  Different versions of X,
different video chips, etc. do this differently.

Meanwhile, recent computer displays (projectors and LCDs) often have
the capability to "resample" the signal, and create a marginally
viewable display out of a signal with a different X by Y size than
the native size of the solid state array of the projector.  However,
this can lead to aliasing, which is where the frequencies don't
quite match, and you get really cruddy pictures.  Worst case
pictures to send to a computer projector that is resampling are
things like grids of alternate black/white pixels or alternate
black/white vertical lines.  

To help the process along, modern projectors and modern laptops
and modern display software are aware of DDC, or display data
channel ( http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Display_Data_Channel )
which communicates available monitor modes from the display to
the computer.  If X is configured correctly, and modern enough,
and the display driver in the kernel permits it, and the phase of
the moon is correct, then the laptop/projector combination "just
works".  However, it doesn't always just work, and sometimes you
have to tweak the resolution of your laptop display to match 
something the projector can handle.  For example, my projector
will resample up to 1280 x 1024 analog video signals, but a 1536
by 1152 signal, or a 1600 by 1200 signal will drive it nuts.  

Some laptops with high native resolution will not "dual head",
and display a higher resolution on their screens than out the 
analog port, so they must be purposely "dumbed down" to display
on a smaller window on the laptop's LCD, in order to match the
projector.  Sometimes it is only possible to get an acceptable
display by dumbing the signal down to the projector's native
resolution, even if the projector will resample from somewhat
higher resolutions, because of aliasing.  This will become
increasingly problematic as the installed computer projector
population ages in relation to the laptops that will be driving
them - downsampling a signal almost always looks crummy, especially
when displaying text, which we Linux command line weenies often do.

In any case, all of this can involve tweaking the Xconfig, typically
while a bunch of impatient people are hammering you with questions
about what you are there to talk about.  Because you are tweaking
on something involving Linux, your surly insistence on focusing on
your tweaking task will be interpreted as a failure of Linux, a
failure of your personality, and failure of the subject you are
there to discuss.  Shortening configuration time with proper tools
is important.  Hence my suggestion for web-based tools to help. 

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs
