From llywrch at agora.rdrop.com  Tue Apr  4 19:31:05 2006
From: llywrch at agora.rdrop.com (Geoffrey Burling)
Date: Tue, 4 Apr 2006 19:31:05 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] befuddled again
In-Reply-To: <200604041233.19194.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <443223CD.5030005@writemoore.net>
	<41017.170.135.112.12.1144171367.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
	<200604041233.19194.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <20060404191916.K83134@agora.rdrop.com>

On Tue, 4 Apr 2006, Eric Wilhelm wrote:

> # from Michael Rasmussen
> # on Tuesday 04 April 2006 10:22 am:
>
>> They are consistant, they don't lie (not even to themselves) and
>> with enough digging you can get to the bottom of any problem.
>
> Unless there's a voltage problem, at which point values that are
> supposed to be binary acquire a "maybe" bit :-)  At least, that's my
> best guess on what was going on with my hard drive problem because it
> definitely isn't the drives.
>
Probably more appropos in this case -- & many others -- than folks 
might consider. Sometimes a powercycle clears up all problems with a 
computer: something in the firmware of a chip somewhere gets corrupted, 
& the only way to clear from memory is a powercycle. Sometimes a *very* 
thorough powercycle: shut the computer off, disconnect the power 
cord & wait 15 seconds for all of the capacitors to discharge.

While Michael is correct, that computers are deterministic beasts, 
each one has enough layers of hardware -> firmware -> driver -> OS -> 
application to permit a stray bitrot to cause havoc up the stack. 
Never underestimate the cleansing strength of a powercycle: it's 
saved Microsoft's butt many times.

On the other hand, unless you can reproduce this glitch on a regular 
basis, don't waste time on it: transient weirdness is part of the 
joys of working with computers. And there are countless more shallow & 
fixable bugs out there to identify & squash. And I look for defects 
in network drivers, BIOSes & other goodies to pay the mortgage, so 
I think I know what I'm talking about. (Hey, I found two bugs in 
ethtool! Well, okay, they were typos in the diagnostic output -- but 
nonetheless I reported them.)

Geoff

Subject: [PLUG] OCR software?

Have had Xsane on my Ubuntu-64 laptop for some time, and it works
beautifully with my Canon LiDE-30 scanner. (I bought the scanner
specifically to use with Linux, so I researched first -- and it all
works perfectly!)

However, now, for the first time, I am trying to OCR some pages of
text. This is linguistics stuff, so I expected that it OCR utility
would choke a bit on IPA characters. But what I am getting is mostly
garbage. It would be faster to type it in de novo. Surely I am doing
something wrong here. 

The first thing that happened was that Xsane gave me an error message
"child process not launched," or something like that. Taking a wild
guess I assumed that this message meant that Xsane could not find an
OCR utility. In other words, installing Xsane included a menu item for
OCR, but not the utility. So I popped up Synaptic and searched on
"optical" and on "OCR." This turned up gocr and ocrad. I installed
gocr, the gocr-doc package and gocr-gtk. Afterwards Xsane was happy to
OCR the image, but the results were lousy. 

The problem is I can't figure out how to tweak gocr. From my experience
with OCR programs on Windows there should be a "learning" tool and
other goodies to enhance the results. For example, I expected a window
to pop up for editing the results, and corrections would be saved to a
learning configuration file somewhere. It should also use whatever
spell checker you have installed as well. But all I get is a prompt for
the output file name.

I assumed gocr-doc was the Help file, but I can't figure out how to
launch it or read it. There are no entries on the gnome panel to launch
gocr or gocr-doc. From the command line I tried gocr, gocr-doc and
gocr-gtk, but all returned just "command not found." There has to be
more to this utility, I just can't figure out how to get into the
options.

Then I installed ocrad, but not only does Xsane not see it, I can't
find any way to launch it. If I type "ocrad" from the command line the
cursor moves down without a prompt in front of it, but nothing appears
on screen and there are no error messages. It looks like something
might be running, but who knows?

As awesome as Xsane is, surely there is something better than this for
OCR. Any suggestions?

considered to be the minimum.

bits to linux and be all set :-)

OCR is not an easy problem.

either interface with gocr, ocrad, or a commercial app (if you want to 
spend $1k on it -- sounds like a $100 donation to gocr or ocrad might 
be a good investment :-/

>launch it or read it. There are no entries on the gnome panel to
> launch gocr or gocr-doc. From the command line I tried gocr, gocr-doc
> and gocr-gtk, but all returned just "command not found."

I'm guessing that ubuntu has dpkg.

  dpkg -L gocr
  dpkg -L gocr | grep bin

might help find it.

At 07:31 PM 4/4/06, you wrote:
>On Tue, 4 Apr 2006, Eric Wilhelm wrote:
>
>># from Michael Rasmussen
>># on Tuesday 04 April 2006 10:22 am:
>>
>>>They are consistant, they don't lie (not even to themselves) and
>>>with enough digging you can get to the bottom of any problem.
>>
>>Unless there's a voltage problem, at which point values that are
>>supposed to be binary acquire a "maybe" bit :-)  At least, that's my
>>best guess on what was going on with my hard drive problem because it
>>definitely isn't the drives.
>Probably more appropos in this case -- & many others -- than folks 
>might consider. Sometimes a powercycle clears up all problems with a 
>computer: something in the firmware of a chip somewhere gets 
>corrupted, & the only way to clear from memory is a powercycle. 
>Sometimes a *very* thorough powercycle: shut the computer off, 
>disconnect the power cord & wait 15 seconds for all of the 
>capacitors to discharge.
>
>While Michael is correct, that computers are deterministic beasts, 
>each one has enough layers of hardware -> firmware -> driver -> OS 
>-> application to permit a stray bitrot to cause havoc up the stack. 
>Never underestimate the cleansing strength of a powercycle: it's 
>saved Microsoft's butt many times.
>
>On the other hand, unless you can reproduce this glitch on a regular 
>basis, don't waste time on it: transient weirdness is part of the 
>joys of working with computers. And there are countless more shallow 
>& fixable bugs out there to identify & squash. And I look for 
>defects in network drivers, BIOSes & other goodies to pay the 
>mortgage, so I think I know what I'm talking about. (Hey, I found 
>two bugs in ethtool! Well, okay, they were typos in the diagnostic 
>output -- but nonetheless I reported them.)
>
>Geoff
>_______________________________________________
>PLUG mailing list
>PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
>http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug



From RParr at TemporalArts.COM  Wed Apr  5 10:28:07 2006
From: RParr at TemporalArts.COM (RParr)

Subject: [PLUG] dual DVI video card
In-Reply-To: <20060404135151.ce9f0088.johnxj@comcast.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

>I was thinking that it might be wise to wait until fall, but from what you say it should be possible to get it all working earlier. I think I'm going to target mid-July for this project, maybe a decent interval after Ubuntu Dapper is out.
>
>  
>

I used wait for the next bleeding wave too (coming this summer...AMD2, 
SocketF, DDR2, nForce5, ...)
but have been tossed up on the beach stunned and bleeding just too many 
times.

I have found the best road to success (as measured in time, frustration, 
actual USE of the computer, ...)
is to target what was hot a few months ago and is now being over 
shadowed by this month's new thing.

For example, AMD X2, Opteron, nForce4, nVidia 78xx, ... have been out 
for a while and were last years big thing
but it is only in the last few months that the requisite fixes/patches 
have filtered through to the standard kernel builds, nVidia drivers, 
etc. and that you can readily build a solid, stable, system without 
getting blood all over yourself.  It's also about the time the prices 
start dropping.  My advice, drool over the Xmas catalogs/magazines, then 
go shopping. :)

R.Parr, RHCE, Temporal Arts
Portland, OR U.S.A.

> See:  http://archie.dotsrc.org/

> Archie is the liveCD version of Arch Linux, but it's a bit behind the 
> times at the moment.  The latest version was released in November '05. 
> It features an XFCE desktop.
>
> Still, it should work.  Arch uses a rolling release system, so in 
> effect the "releases" are just snapshots of the distro at a particular 
> time.  I think just about everybody who uses Arch tracks the CURRENT 
> repositories (and the adventurous also track the TESTING 
> repositories), which means as long as you're installing software then 
> your system is always up-to-date.  That also means that November '05, 
> in Arch Linux terms, is ancient history.  It seems that a lot of the 
> XFCE fans on the Arch message boards are using SVN builds of the next 
> XFCE release, which are already packaged for Arch.  These folks live 
> on the edge. :-)
>
> BTW, the hardware detection tool (hwd is the name, I think) that 
> Archie developers built for the live CD is also included in the main 
> distro now and it's excellent.  It created an almost perfect xorg.conf 
> file for me in about half-a-second.  The only thing wrong with it was 
> a missing screen resolution, but that was easily fixed.  It's even 
> better than Debian's 'dpkg-reconfigure xorg', which I thought was 
> already a big improvement over 'xorg -configure'.
>

# from John Jason Jordan
# on Wednesday 05 April 2006 11:31 am:

>> I'm guessing that you might want to scan the page to a (pbm) file
>> and work from that while you're trying to get the OCR working
>> just-so. ?If xsane runs the scanner every time you try this, that
>> extended wait is going to be frustrating.
>
>The problem is that Xsane -- as cool as it is -- seems to have no
>ability to OCR from a PBM file (whatever that is -- something like a
>TIFF?). I can save to a PBM file from Xsane. It's just that a PBM file
>is all that it will remain.

My suggestion is that you save a pbm from the scan and then work-out the 
options or configs for the ocr from the command-line (gocr [options] 
pnm_file_name).  Once you have that working, you'll need to find a way 
to set the options in xsane or kooka.  But first you have to figure out 
how to tweak gocr and that is going to require reading the manual and 
docs and figuring out what the -m 130 option does.

The ascii art is awesome, but it could use a gui frontend for the 
training mode.  Given the current figures, 
 ( development cost / my annual profit on scanner sales => infinity )
it's going to be a while before I write one.

References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604051427510.29815@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <44345718.5000502@writemoore.net>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   My new MSI K7N2 Delta2 motherboard is built around the nVidia nForce2
> Gigabit MCP/RAID MCP. It includes, "integrated hardware Sound 
> Blaster/Direct
> Sound AC97 audio."
> 
>   /sbin/lspci reports: "Multimedia audio controller: nVidia Corporation 
> MCP2S
> AC'97 Audio Controller (rev a1)." I wonder if I have the proper modules
> built.
> 
>   Here's what's in /lib/modules/kernel/drivers/sound:
> 
> ac97.o                cs4232.o      maui.o           sgalaxy.o
> ac97_codec.o          cs4281/       mpu401.o         sonicvibes.o
> ac97_plugin_ad1980.o  cs46xx.o      msnd.o           sound.o
> ac97_plugin_wm97xx.o  emu10k1/      msnd_classic.o   soundcore.o
> aci.o                 es1370.o      msnd_pinnacle.o  sscape.o
> ad1816.o              es1371.o      nm256_audio.o    trident.o
> ad1848.o              esssolo1.o    opl3.o           trix.o
> ad1889.o              forte.o       opl3sa.o         uart401.o
> adlib_card.o          gus.o         opl3sa2.o        uart6850.o
> aedsp16.o             i810_audio.o  pas2.o           v_midi.o
> ali5455.o             kahlua.o      pss.o            via82cxxx_audio.o
> awe_wave.o            mad16.o       rme96xx.o        wavefront.o
> btaudio.o             maestro.o     sb.o             ymfpci.o
> cmpci.o               maestro3.o    sb_lib.o
> 
>   lsmod reports:
> 
> sound                  55624   0 (unused)
> soundcore               3652   2 [sound]
> ac97_plugin_wm97xx     12704   0 (unused)
> ac97_codec             13268   0 [ac97_plugin_wm97xx]
> 
>   What am I missing?

My understanding of these things is pretty limited, but if you are using 
alsa, don't you need the alsa driver loaded?  The alsa matrix reports 
that nVidia nForce chipsets use the intel8x0 driver:

http://www.alsa-project.org/alsa-doc/index.php?vendor=vendor-Nvidia#matrix

That would be "i810audio.o" from your list.

>From the man wget page:
       --no-passive-ftp
           Disable the use of the passive FTP transfer mode.  Passive
FTP mandates that the client connect to the server to establish the data
connection rather than the other way around.

           If the machine is connected to the Internet directly, both
passive and active FTP should work equally well.  Behind most firewall
and NAT configurations passive FTP has a better chance of working.
However, in some rare firewall configurations, active FTP actually works
when passive FTP doesn?t.  If you suspect this to be the case, use this
option, or set "passive_ftp=off" in your init file.

I think the command you want is "sudo ifdown wlan0." The final 
character is a zero. To turn it back on replace "down" with "up."

At 04:23 PM 4/5/06, you wrote:
>Jim Karlock wrote:
>>I see some possibilities not mentioned so far (unless I missed it):
>>1. Something changed in the modem. I worked around my problem by 
>>removing the modem from the list of addresses in Linus and pointing 
>>primary and secondary addresses to the ISP.
>
>I'm sorry, could you be a little more more expansive?  What do you 
>mean, "removing the modem from the list of addresses"?

OK, I am new to Linux and forgot the details. I just checked the 
linux box to get the menu sequence I used with FC4:
   Click desktop at the top of the main screen
     Select system settings from the drop down menu
       Select the DNS tab on the box that opened.
         I found the primary DNS address set to 192.168.0.1, the modem.
         The secondary DNS was set to my ISP

What I did to slove the problem was to put the DNS address of the ISP 
in both places by copying the secondary DNS address to the primary 
DNS address. Another machine with Qwest as ISP started working when I 
got the primary and secondary DNS address from their support number.

Now the rest of the story:
As I mentioned, I just checked the Linux box to get the information 
to write the above. I found that the Modem's address had re-appeared 
as the Primary DNS. But it still works.

As I originally said, something in the modem or the ISP is suggested 
by the data.

Hope this helps someone.

Thanks
JK

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Thu Apr  6 09:08:25 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Thu, 06 Apr 2006 09:08:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] turn off a wireless card 
In-Reply-To: Message from Russell Senior <seniorr@aracnet.com> 
	of "06 Apr 2006 00:02:05 PDT." <86hd57qj9u.fsf@coulee.tdb.com> 
Message-ID: <20060406160830.EE48A8F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:

> >>>>> "Galen" == Galen Seitz <galens at seitzassoc.com> writes:
> 
> Galen> The Intel 2200BG shows up as eth1, not wlan0, on my system.
> Galen> "ifdown eth1" stops any packets from being routed over the
> Galen> wireless link, but the LED wireless indicator still goes on and
> Galen> off as if the radio is still active.  iwconfig gives no
> Galen> indication that the radio is off.
> 
> Did you try:
> 
>   iwconfig eth1 power off

I think that controls how often the radio sleeps.  It may be possible to
turn off the radio that way, but the command would have to be 
iwconfig eth1 power on
I tried it anyway, but it didn't seem to have any effect.

The rfswitch web site looked promising, but the X40 is shown as 'None'
in the laptop support table.

I found what I was looking for in the readme for the ipw2200 driver.


1.4.2 Device Level Sysfs Helper Files
-----------------------------------------------

For the device level files, look in
	
	/sys/bus/pci/drivers/ipw2200/{PCI-ID}/

For example:
	/sys/bus/pci/drivers/ipw2200/0000:02:01.0

For the device level files, see /sys/bus/pci/drivers/ipw2200:

  rf_kill
	read - 
	0 = RF kill not enabled (radio on)
	1 = SW based RF kill active (radio off)
	2 = HW based RF kill active (radio off)
	3 = Both HW and SW RF kill active (radio off)
	write -
	0 = If SW based RF kill active, turn the radio back on
	1 = If radio is on, activate SW based RF kill

	NOTE: If you enable the SW based RF kill and then toggle the HW
  	based RF kill from ON -> OFF -> ON, the radio will NOT come back on


On my X40 the following command turns off the rf, or at least I think it
does, as the LED never changes state after the command is issued.
  echo 1 > /sys/bus/pci/drivers/ipw2200/0000\:02\:02.0/rf_kill


galen

From nixlists at writemoore.net  Thu Apr  6 10:04:33 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Thu, 06 Apr 2006 10:04:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] more on Tbird compacting
Message-ID: <44354A21.2090905@writemoore.net>

Regarding all the prior discussion about Mozilla Thunderbird and the 
compacting of mail folders, I upgraded to Thunderbird 1.5 and the 
compacting option is now located under the "Advanced" tab of Preferences 
panel, on the sub-tab "Offline and Disk Space."  Thunderbird 1.5, like 
Firefox 1.5, has redone the layout of the Preferences panel, for the 
better I think.  One nice feature is that it provides easy access from 
Preferences to "about:config," the place where you can really go to town 
tweaking Thunderbird to your heart's content.  It's also under the 
Advanced tab, at the bottom of the "General" sub-tab.  There's a button 
called "Config Editor" that opens up about:config, which contains all 
the hidden preference options.

But about the compacting, I think Tbird does it automatically now.  I 
noticed it compacting a folder (it displays a message in the status 
bar), so I went hunting for the setting.  Sure enough, the compacting 
box was unchecked.  So even though I hadn't told it to compact, it was 
doing it anyway.

> 
> OK, I am new to Linux and forgot the details. I just checked the linux

>   Click desktop at the top of the main screen
>     Select system settings from the drop down menu
>       Select the DNS tab on the box that opened.
>         I found the primary DNS address set to 192.168.0.1, the modem.
>         The secondary DNS was set to my ISP
> 
> What I did to slove the problem was to put the DNS address of the ISP in 
> both places by copying the secondary DNS address to the primary DNS 
> address. Another machine with Qwest as ISP started working when I got 
> the primary and secondary DNS address from their support number.
> 
> Now the rest of the story:
> As I mentioned, I just checked the Linux box to get the information to 
> write the above. I found that the Modem's address had re-appeared as the 
> Primary DNS. But it still works.
> 
> As I originally said, something in the modem or the ISP is suggested by 
> the data.
> 

OIC what you mean.  You're talking about DNS, but my problems were not 
DNS related.  An easy way to tell if you're having DNS issues is to 
watch the status bar of your browser.  When you enter a URL or click on 
a link, the status bar will show "Looking for..." the site.  If it hangs 
there (i.e., never finds the site), you might be having DNS problems. 
If it progresses to "Waiting for..." the site, then the DNS look-up 
succeeded and it is trying to download the appropriate page.

Of course, there are more sophisticated ways of checking DNS, like 
nslookup (read 'man nslookup').  But just watching the browser status 
bar is a good first clue.

In my case, I didn't have any problems finding the servers.  I just 
couldn't connect to them successfully once I did find them.

Thanks anyway for the explanation.

>>From the man wget page:
>       --no-passive-ftp
>           Disable the use of the passive FTP transfer mode.  Passive
>FTP mandates that the client connect to the server to establish the data
>connection rather than the other way around.
>
>           If the machine is connected to the Internet directly, both
>passive and active FTP should work equally well.  Behind most firewall
>and NAT configurations passive FTP has a better chance of working.
>However, in some rare firewall configurations, active FTP actually works
>when passive FTP doesn?t.  If you suspect this to be the case, use this
>option, or set "passive_ftp=off" in your init file.
>

Linux related, though a bit commercially tainted :-/

Until midnight Thursday 4/06 (eastern time?) Syngress Books is
selling off a bunch of titles at high discounts, some $5 each,
plus shipping.  They have a lot of books on security, many for
open source apps.

A week ago, I bought a copy of Wotring's "Host Intregrity Monitoring 
Using Osiris and Samhain" for $10 + $4.52 DHL shipping.  It is now
$5 (List $45).   They also provide a free PDF download with the dead
tree version, which is cheaper than the E-book version.  Go figure.
I guess they really, really need money to pay taxes.

Note - two annoyances. First:  the book was poorly trimmed;  I had
to cut open twenty or so page pairs with my trusty pocket knife. 
So trim your Syngress book before going to the airport.

Second, the book was sent "cheapest way" with DHL, which meant that
although it arrived last Friday, they weren't planning on delivering
it until Wednesday, the estimated delivery time.  That's just petty.
However, I was down to the DHL office to ship out a warranty repair,
so the kind folks behind the counter dug the book out for me.

Again, forgive a dollar denominated posting, but many of the books
are about Linux!

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Apr  6 19:40:22 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 6 Apr 2006 19:40:22 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Need Help w/Sound: ThinkPad 600E
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604061928280.4568@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I've had great help here on identifying the missing sound driver for my new
workstation. Now I'm stuck on getting sound working on my fiancee's ThinkPad
600E.

   On the ALSA project Web site's Trouble Shooting page, I worked my way from
the top down. Running alsaconf revealed the incorrect driver was loaded. The
widget searched the system (reading lspci?), indentified the correct Cirrus
Logic chip, found the driver, and modifed /etc/modules.conf as well as
running modprobe to load the correct driver and its helper friends. That
checked out OK with alsamixer, both as root and as a user. When I let the
ALSA widget find, install, and configure the proper driver, it also removed
~/.asoundrc. But, the directory /proc/asound/ contained all the proper
information (e.g., found the card, correct alsa version).

   Every step checked out in the series "drivers loaded, but still no sound."
I'm trying to run xmms on that box, and I know that uses the ALSA drivers,
not the OSS drivers, so I stopped before that section.

   When I run xmms, I see the mixer showing the sound being generated. I've
run aumix and turned up the sound levels for master and PGM to about 80
percent; that should make a loud noise. No sound.

   In summary, the alsa tools found the correct drivers and loaded them. Sound
channels are open according to aumix and alsamixer. The application xmms is
doing its thing. There's still no sound to be heard.

   I'd like some thoughts from you more experienced folks on what else I need
to check, or what I may have missed here.

TIA,

Rich

thinkpad here, t40 series, had linux up and running in half a day, but no 
sound. found "thinkwiki" - took me two weeks of searching to discover that you 
had to MUTE two channels in order to get the speakers to work - it's 
explicitly mentioned on the alsa FAQ too.

> explicitly mentioned on the alsa FAQ too.

> I believe it's "line jack sense" and "headphone jack sense".

Auke,

   That must be one of the differences between the 600E and T40. In both
alsamixer and aumix, the only unmuted channels are master and PGM. There is
no "line jack sense" or "headphone jack sense" in either of those
applications.

   The ALSA wiki trouble shooting page mentioned switching between analog and
digital speaker modes, but the information for the driver on that box had no
indication there was a choice, or any way of selecting them.

   I'll look more tomorrow.

Thanks,

Rich

From aaron at bavariati.org  Fri Apr  7 10:19:09 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Fri, 7 Apr 2006 10:19:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Box
In-Reply-To: <1144388352.5358.252.camel@localhost.localdomain>
References: <1144388352.5358.252.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Thu, Apr 06, 2006 at 10:39:11PM -0700, Vram wrote:
> I inherited this Linux box at work...
> It is currently hosting 5 websites...

Oh joy, an old, exposed system.  Backup data, then run chkrootkit.

> Problem # 1
[what is it?]
> What else should I check for?

Try: 
 cat /etc/issue
 cat /etc/*-version
 cat /etc/*-release

> Problem # 2
> 
> Filesystem            Size  Used Avail Use% Mounted on
> /dev/sda2             3.7G  3.5G   60M  99% /

Try: 
 du / -x --max-depth=1 
Check /root, /tmp, /var/tmp and /var/log for stuff you can get rid of.

Make sure the data is safe.  Looks like it's all on a single SCSI disc,
so make a backup YESTERDAY.  From a box nearby with enough free space, do: 
 ssh root at web_server "tar -zc /" > web_server.tar.gz

> Problem #3 
> 
> ON reboot only ETH0 comes up

If it's redhat, check /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-eth0:0 for an
"onboot=yes" or some such.

> Thanks for the help

Let us know how it goes,
  Aaron

Michael M. wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> I went to grab a live CD so I could test drive it and found them 
>> lacking in this offering...shucks!!
>>
>
> See:  http://archie.dotsrc.org/
>
> Archie is the liveCD version of Arch Linux, but it's a bit behind the 
> times at the moment.  The latest version was released in November '05. 
> It features an XFCE desktop.
>
> Still, it should work.  Arch uses a rolling release system, so in 
> effect the "releases" are just snapshots of the distro at a particular 
> time.  I think just about everybody who uses Arch tracks the CURRENT 
> repositories (and the adventurous also track the TESTING 
> repositories), which means as long as you're installing software then 
> your system is always up-to-date.  That also means that November '05, 
> in Arch Linux terms, is ancient history.  It seems that a lot of the 
> XFCE fans on the Arch message boards are using SVN builds of the next 
> XFCE release, which are already packaged for Arch.  These folks live 
> on the edge. :-)
>
> BTW, the hardware detection tool (hwd is the name, I think) that 
> Archie developers built for the live CD is also included in the main 
> distro now and it's excellent.  It created an almost perfect xorg.conf 
> file for me in about half-a-second.  The only thing wrong with it was 
> a missing screen resolution, but that was easily fixed.  It's even 
> better than Debian's 'dpkg-reconfigure xorg', which I thought was 
> already a big improvement over 'xorg -configure'.
>

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Fri Apr  7 13:07:14 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)

Subject: [PLUG] Disto question
In-Reply-To: <4436B95D.7060604@verizon.net>
References: <4436B95D.7060604@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <14b324ee72b82bd43aec8bf19f576bce@localhost>



some hints on boot problems -

some old boxes don't like the iso boot sequence with 2.88 emulated floppies (el torito). Most of them prefer an upgraded bios - I've succesfully booted p200 boxes that seemed completely dead at first this way (wouldn't even start loading the kernel e.g.)

always assume your bios is broken first, nobody back in the days knew how to implement acpi properly and the bios tables often are futzed. While windows will load (because it just remaps everything) linux might fail (it actually tries to follow the bios info). So good hints are to disable acpi and apic (boot with noapic noacpi for starters). 

I'm sure there are more tricks but these are the ones that get most of my systems going.

Auke

> On my 600E, I used the OSS driver "cs4232". This is the relevant kernel
> option:

   Thanks, Evan. I had tried the cs4232 module (based on extensive reports
found on Google. Then I let alsaconf do its thing, and it found a different
driver.

   Apparently I now have some OSS drivers and some ALSA drivers, but not
enough of either. So, I'm compiling a new kernel (2.4.32) and will start over
again.

   The reports turned up on Google searches are really confusing. A lot of
folks report success with the cs4232 driver. Others (I think on the ALSA
site) say that it's the 4636 driver, despite the system reporting it needs
the 46xx driver.

Many thanks,

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604061928280.4568@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604061928280.4568@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <1144447782.9026.5.camel@luffy>

On Thu, 2006-04-06 at 19:40 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    I've had great help here on identifying the missing sound driver for my new
> workstation. Now I'm stuck on getting sound working on my fiancee's ThinkPad
> 600E.

Rich, 

On my 600E, I used the OSS driver "cs4232". This is the relevant kernel
option:

 Symbol: SOUND_CS4232 [=m]                                              
 Prompt: Crystal CS4232 based (PnP) cards                               
   Defined at sound/oss/Kconfig:600                                     
   Depends on: !M68K && SOUND!=n && (BROKEN || !SPARC32 && !SPARC64) && 
   Location:                                                            
     -> Device Drivers                                                  
       -> Sound                                                         
         -> Open Sound System                                           
           -> Open Sound System (DEPRECATED) (SOUND_PRIME [=m])          
             -> OSS sound modules (SOUND_OSS [=m])   

As I recall, it has to be compiled as a module, and it needs to be
unloaded and reloaded at runtime in order for sound to play.

Good luck,

-Evan

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Apr  8 11:48:27 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 8 Apr 2006 11:48:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Scanning and OCR in Linux
Message-ID: <20060408114827.602c36db.johnxj@comcast.net>

Scanning works better than under Windows. OCR sucks rocks.

As far as I can tell there are only two OCR "engines" for Linux, gocr and ocrad. Both might work better if I could get them to use a dictionary. I can't find any way to get ocrad to use a dictionary at all. Gocr will, but only under Kooka (not XSane), and it won't use any dictionary that I have installed. That's because you have to choose the dictionary in a drop-down box that you can't type into, and none of the OO.o dictionaries are listed.  Plus there is a bug in Kooka that requires you to close and restart the program for each page you OCR.

I do realize the OCR software is incredibly complicated. Outfits like Aabbyy, OmniPage, and others have spent many milliions of dollars on programmer salaries to develop their OCR engines. 

One of my big complaints, however, is that, even if the OCR engine worked well, you can't get it to work because the GUI is not implemented correctly. In Kooka, for example, you can click on a button and sometimes absolutely nothing happens. What passes for documentation sometimes doesn't even explain what a button is supposed to do. As far as I can tell the programmer just put some of the buttons on the interface and never got around to writing the code to make them do anything.

So are there any other Linux OCR choices besides ocrad and gocr? (I include command line tools, as long as I can figure out how to use them; i.e., they come with understandable documentation.) Or are there any commercial OCR utilities that will run under Linux? Or should I hire a typist to keyboard stuff into the computer for me?

Right now I have used XSane (a very nice utility) to scan the 35 pages I need to TIFF files. XSane does a great job. I used TIFF because I know Windows OCR utilities can OCR from a TIFF without needing a scanner. So, push come to shove, I can move the TIFFs to the Windows computer and finish up there. But there must be something that works under Linux. Someone please help me be a Linux purist.

far along it is.

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Sat Apr  8 13:26:20 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Sat, 08 Apr 2006 13:26:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Disto question
In-Reply-To: <44381935.5060503@verizon.net>
References: <442F03D3.3000401@verizon.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0604011520300.6644@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<442F5FE9.3020404@verizon.net>	<53107f6e0604021312h5d4f6b52n4801b702176b31c8@mail.gmail.com>	<4430733F.10503@writemoore.net>	<53107f6e0604031306w67b2ca5bo6ef645cc8fa19cba@mail.gmail.com>	<44318319.5080600@verizon.net>	<4431889D.30807@writemoore.net>	<4436B95D.7060604@verizon.net>
	<44381757.1030404@writemoore.net> <44381935.5060503@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <44381C6C.5070209@foo-projects.org>

Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
> Hopefully someone else can answer that question.  It might be i686.  I 
> had a 5x86 that was nothing more than an over clocked 486 (both AMD) and 
> it was a smokin machine for it's time.
> 
> More research necessary.

486 != 586 - they are really different instruction sets making them different. 
If you run generic x86 code (==i386) it will work (of course) but back then 
nobody was using -march=i586 anyway.

> The fact of the matter is that I am experimenting to see what the old 
> girl will run.  At this point in time she is exhausting herself running 
> XPPro...she's tough but struggling with the newer software.  More memory 
> would certainly not hurt...Did someone say Free Geek?


run `arch` on it and it will show the correct value:

$ arch
i586

k6 and k6-2 are not 100% i686. In theory most i686 software will work - 
especially if generically compiled with "sane" optimizations. But at random 
intervals you may experience the horrible "Illegal Instruction" errors.

Auke

In the meantime, I have been googling on OCR and linux and discovered a few things, the most significant of which is that abbyy has released a development kit for Linux, and evidently it is free:

http://www.abbyy.com/for_developers/?param=28804

In the Windows world, abbyy's  FineReader is considered the best OCR software for the individual desktop. There are others that run a close second, but FineReader is held as the one to beat. So now it's time for the Linux proggers to create an interface for the abbyy OCR engine.

In the meantime, I guess I have to rely on Windows to do OCR.

arch reports my K6II 350 as i586.



Auke Kok wrote:

> Bruce Kilpatrick wrote:
>
>> Hopefully someone else can answer that question.  It might be i686.  
>> I had a 5x86 that was nothing more than an over clocked 486 (both 
>> AMD) and it was a smokin machine for it's time.
>>
>> More research necessary.
>
>
> 486 != 586 - they are really different instruction sets making them 
> different. If you run generic x86 code (==i386) it will work (of 
> course) but back then nobody was using -march=i586 anyway.
>
>> The fact of the matter is that I am experimenting to see what the old 
>> girl will run.  At this point in time she is exhausting herself 
>> running XPPro...she's tough but struggling with the newer software.  
>> More memory would certainly not hurt...Did someone say Free Geek?
>
>
>
> run `arch` on it and it will show the correct value:
>
> $ arch
> i586
>
> k6 and k6-2 are not 100% i686. In theory most i686 software will work 
> - especially if generically compiled with "sane" optimizations. But at 
> random intervals you may experience the horrible "Illegal Instruction" 
> errors.
>
> Auke
>
>

   I managed to miss specifying generic_serial when I built my custom kernels
(2.4.32 and 2.6.16). I need that driver for the pci serial card (the
motherboard has only one serial port on it). It's been so long since I built
a single driver as a module.

   One reference found on Google suggests that I use:
gcc -DMODULE -D__KERNEL__ -O6 -c generic_serial.c
but that results in:

generic_serial.c:1083: error: syntax error before
"this_object_must_be_defined_as_export_objs_in_the_Makefile"
generic_serial.c:1083: warning: data definition has no type or storage class

"this_object_must_be_defined_as_export_objs_in_the_Makefile"
generic_serial.c:1084: warning: data definition has no type or storage class
generic_serial.c:1085: error: syntax error before
"this_object_must_be_defined_as_export_objs_in_the_Makefile"

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sun Apr  9 21:17:38 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)

Subject: [PLUG] Scanning and OCR in Linux

Message-ID: <20060409211738.7a0e459f.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Sun, 9 Apr 2006 19:37:05 -0700
Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org> dijo:

> On Sat, Apr 08, 2006 at 09:43:45PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > In the meantime, I guess I have to rely on Windows to do OCR. 
> 
> Shame, that.  The state of voice-recognition is pretty poor, too.
> Reading the Oregonian's "Ooh! Shiny! Windows Vista!" article, that was
> the one feature that wasn't already available on Linux.
> 
> Wine has a SANE-TWAIN interface, so Winders apps can use scanners in
> Linux.  I wonder if that could be made to work with an OCR app?  Might
> be a fun project for some Linux Clinic or something.

I later found the following site:

http://www.simpleocr.com/Demo/

If you upload a scan from XSane as a black and white TIFF at about 300 dpi it does a pretty good job on the bare text. Unlike the sophisticated OCR utilities available on Windows, however, it blows away pictures and even tables. Can't even handle tabs or columns. It also has no "learning" abilities. But it's better than retyping. And I used it for the 35 pages I needed to do, so I was able to avoid having to use Windows.

Pehaps a web-based interface is the future for OCR on Linux? Maybe it could be made a feature of OpenOffice.org? That way, while we're hitting Bill Gates in his cash cow, MS Office, we can hit Abbyy (FineReader) and Nuance (Omnipage) at the same time.

>    I managed to miss specifying generic_serial when I built my custom kernels
> (2.4.32 and 2.6.16). I need that driver for the pci serial card (the
> motherboard has only one serial port on it). It's been so long since I built

I would just do another make menuconfig or its equivalent and enable the
additional driver, followed by make modules.  Then you can copy the module
to the appropriate spot in /lib/modules.  It's probably safe to do
make modules_install, but I won't guarantee it.

I know there's a more direct way, but a quick search didn't find it.

galen

> I would just do another make menuconfig or its equivalent and enable the
> additional driver, followed by make modules. Then you can copy the module

   Here are what I think are the relevant options, as found in .config:

CONFIG_SERIAL=y
CONFIG_SERIAL_CONSOLE=y
CONFIG_SERIAL_EXTENDED=y
CONFIG_SERIAL_MANY_PORTS=y
CONFIG_SERIAL_SHARE_IRQ=y
# CONFIG_SERIAL_DETECT_IRQ is not set
# CONFIG_SERIAL_MULTIPORT is not set
# CONFIG_SERIAL_NONSTANDARD is not set

   Everything else relates to USB serial converters. Might I want to set
SERIA_MULTIPORT to get the generic_serial module built? (There's nothing
relevant when I grep for GENERIC in .config.)

speaker on our alarm clock is apparently always powered, so if we
leave one of our phones near it overnight we get awakened rather
rudely.

do with the periodic (once an hour?) "ping" between the phone and the
tower.  I would have thought they would ping more often than that, but
what do I know.

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Mon Apr 10 13:40:11 2006
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Mon, 10 Apr 2006 13:40:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Compiling/Installing a Single Driver
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604101209590.14850@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <20060410165123.DCA5C8F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0604101209590.14850@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840604101340la87ec85jd569ab5034a0b558@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/10/06, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 10 Apr 2006, Galen Seitz wrote:
>
> >> # CONFIG_SERIAL_DETECT_IRQ is not set
> >> # CONFIG_SERIAL_MULTIPORT is not set
> >> # CONFIG_SERIAL_NONSTANDARD is not set
>
> > If you use make menuconfig, you'll have the benefit of the help text that
> > describes some of the options.
>
> galen,
>
>    That gave me no insight. The help described intelligent serial boards and
> non-standard boards. Nothing that clued me in to what builds
> generic_serial.c. So, I keep looking.

Rich -

generic_serial.c is enabled by enabling one of the drivers that
requires it.  Look in drivers/char/Makefile to see which drivers
enable generic_serial.  You will have to turn on at least one of those
CONFIG_* options in your .config.

NOTE: I am looking at a 2.6 kernel, I am not sure how much of this
applies to 2.4.

Jason

obj-$(CONFIG_MVME147_SCC) += generic_serial.o vme_scc.o
obj-$(CONFIG_MVME162_SCC) += generic_serial.o vme_scc.o

selected for building.

    Part Time Network/System Admin - Catholic Grade School

All Saints School is a Pre-K through 8 Catholic school located in 
Northeast Portland. It has a network of about 75 computers, a 
combination of Windows clients, Windows and Linux servers, which are 
used for administration, education, and general office use. All Saints 
is planning to hire a part-time network support person to provide the 
following functions:
? Hardware and software troubleshooting (hardware repair not required)
? Hardware and software installation
? User and email account management
? Network support
? Linux web and file server management
? Assistance to teachers and staff in use of technology
? Printer queue configuration and management

Additional tasks that might be undertaken by this person, based on 
available time and skills, are:
? Web site design, maintenance and updates
? User training classes for teachers and staff
? Assistance with the All Saints Student Computer Club

Candidates for this position should have the following training and 
experience:
? Two or more years of help-desk and/or general network support
? Familiarity with Windows 2000/XP and Microsoft Office
? Familiarity with backup and restore procedures
? Familiarity with installing and configuring desktop applications such 
as anti-virus tools and email clients
? Familiarity with thin-client/server configuration and administration
? Experience with diagnosing common user problems (network problems, 
printing problems, changing passwords, etc.)
? Experience managing a Windows Network using Active Directory
? Familiarity with (or willingness to learn) Linux and Apache 
administration

The candidate should have excellent communication skills, particularly 
with non-technical users, and should be available for sixteen hours per 
week on a daytime schedule. This schedule will be arranged for mutual 
convenience, but must include time on-site at least two days per week 
between 8:00 AM and 4:30 PM.

    * Job location is NE Portland
    * Compensation: depending on experience
    * This is a part-time job.
    * This is at a non-profit organization.

From m0gely at telestream.com  Tue Apr 11 09:44:00 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Tue, 11 Apr 2006 09:44:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wget -c
In-Reply-To: <443BD976.5000109@rocksolidservice.com>
References: <443BD976.5000109@rocksolidservice.com>
Message-ID: <443BDCD0.4050308@telestream.com>

Christian wrote:
> I need to download my entire site to a new machine so that I can manage 
> it. I believe that the command is...
> wget -c www.mysite.com
> but how do I send it to a specific folder? So that I can find it...
> 
> Also, where does it go if I have just entered in the command above?

I think you mean 'wget -m' for mirror.  It will make a folder 
automatically based on the name of the site where ever you run the 
command from and put everything in there.

'wget --help' is your friend.

From cbrink at brinkrods.com  Tue Apr 11 10:27:14 2006
From: cbrink at brinkrods.com (Christian Brink)
Date: Tue, 11 Apr 2006 10:27:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wget -c
In-Reply-To: <443BD976.5000109@rocksolidservice.com>
References: <443BD976.5000109@rocksolidservice.com>
Message-ID: <443BE6F2.1000509@brinkrods.com>

Christian wrote:
> I need to download my entire site to a new machine so that I can manage 
> it. I believe that the command is...
> wget -c www.mysite.com
> but how do I send it to a specific folder? So that I can find it...

That command will not do what you want it to. '-c' is for continuing a 
failed download.

But I would recommend against doing a site grab, it does not guarantee 
you will get all the files. You'll only get files that have a link from 
another page on the site.

Your best option is to Tar the directory on the original server and 
download that. If you're being hosted and do not have cli access, I 
would ask the tech support to Tar it up for you - it only takes a few 
seconds.


> Also, where does it go if I have just entered in the command above?

It would go to the current directory.


Christian Brink

Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0604111630uf747ad8s492b7891211cb8ce@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/10/06, Piet van Weel <pmvw at wbcable.net> wrote:
> Hey guys...
>
> Interesting question here for you. (Well actually two of them.)
>
> Does anybody else encounter severe AM interference when dealing with
> wireless devices? (aka laptops, bluetooth devices, etc.)
>
> Does anybody randomly encounter a dah-dah-de-dit-dit on their radio?
>
> Anybody know what that is, or know how to counter it?

There  was discussion on Slashdot about this recently:
http://ask.slashdot.org/article.pl?sid=06/04/07/2111217

Apparently TDMA (and thus GSM and iDEN/Nextel) devices frequently do
this. The solution is to move the phone further away or shield better.

I've personally experienced this frequently with GSM or Nextel phones
and cheap computer speakers.

Dan Young


From nixlists at writemoore.net  Tue Apr 11 18:40:20 2006
From: nixlists at writemoore.net (Michael M.)
Date: Tue, 11 Apr 2006 18:40:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Help me help a hapless WinXP user
Message-ID: <443C5A84.3030500@writemoore.net>

I have a friend in New York that I'm collaborating on something with.  
Most of what we need to do we can do through email, but it's getting to 
the point where it would be really useful for me to be able to transfer 
files to (and sometimes retrieve from) his computer by other means.  He 
has a recent Dell running Windows XP Home.

The thing is, my friend ... well, how do I put this?  He was a 
successful Wall Street stockbroker for about 15 years.  He speaks 
English, French and German fluently, and Spanish and Italian passably.  
He has a B.A. in English Lit from Williams College and an M.A. in Comp. 
Lit from NYU.  He's a great cook and a creative and capable gardener.  
Put him in front of a computer, and your typical 4-year-old could 
probably manage better than he can.  He can do the basics of word 
processing -- by that I mean, he can type on a keyboard and the words 
will appear in the document, which he is capable of saving.  He can just 
about manage to use AOL, though not without some difficulty.  A typical 
statement is, "I think I downloaded something, but I don't know where it 
went."  At one point, I created a folder on his desktop which I named 
"DOWNLOADS" and I directed any and all downloads from any AOL source 
there.  He still couldn't find them.  I had him using Ubuntu for about 
six months and he did as well there as he does in Windows -- which is to 
say, he just about got by.  I did everything that might loosely be 
termed "administration" for him.

I really don't know much of anything about Windows to Linux networking, 
and since I don't have a Windows machine at my disposal I can't 
experiment.  Is there something that I could walk him through the set-up 
of that would enable me to move files back and forth without having to 
depend upon him doing anything except providing me with his IP address 
if it changes?  Setting up an ftp server on his machine would probably 
test our friendship, I was hoping for an easier solution.  I found 
copSSH, which doesn't look too difficult, though by default it would 
restrict me to a single directory.  Getting him to move what I need to 
that directory would be an on-going hassle, given that he just doesn't 
grasp the concept of "directories."  Ideally, I would be able to have 
any and all remote-desktop capabilities, but I think Windows only 
provides that from one Windows machine to another, and even then only in 
XP Professional.  You would think, given how often Windows machines get 
p'wned, that there ought to be an easy way to voluntarily hand over 
privileges to someone you want to be able to access your machine.  
Anyone have any suggestions?

TIA

-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA
"No live organism can continue for long to exist sanely under conditions of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to dream." --S. Jackson

I had forgotten how annoying this feature is with phones that employ TDMA. I 
recall years ago with my old analog phone it would cause my computer speakers 
to make noise anytime an incoming call came in. I had CDMA service for 
several years and it never affected my speakers but recently I switched over 
to GSM service with Cingular and so I'm back to having speaker noise.

I even managed to annoy everyone in a meeting one day when my phone was 
causing the speaker within the tabletop speaker-phone to make a bunch of 
noise even though it wasn't online with a call. I had to move my phone 
several feet away before the noise finally stopped. :(

Here is a fun one for you... :)

For about two years I was in charge of the media aspects of my church. 
Which meant that every Sunday I was sitting in front of the old analog 
sound board, video presentation, and light controller. My timer for 
church service that I used was my Cingular PDA phone. It was usually 
placed on top of the sound board so we could see the time. Unfortunately 
quite often during the middle of the church service the sound 
interference would get broadcast throughout the entire church!!

So now cellular phones are not allowed in the sound booth!!

On Wed, Apr 12, 2006 at 08:50:54AM -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   I've tried to send an email message with .pdf attachments to someone at an
> organization that runs only Microsoft. Apparently, whatever malware detector
> they've installed and configured sees that file extension as something
> dangersous, so the messages are not delivered to the intended recipient. I'm
> not the only one whose mail with innocuous attachments ends up in the bit

Yes to both.  I haven't the faintest idea why they'd block PDFs, but
basically everything useful Winders users would want to attach to email
(.DOC, .XLS, .ZIP, .JPG) has been used to carry viruses/worms.

I've seen mail setups that filter most attachments, put 'em on a web
server and substitute an URL in the message.  Also helps with those
pesky "Ooh!  Everyone look at this 10MB video!" email storms.

the company to share information and programs from multiple sites.

What we did was rename the file without the extension, send that file
and tell the user on the other end to save and rename it.
Worked well because the filtering system didn't inspect the contents and
the e-mail virus scanners won't (will they?) find anything wrong with the file.

On Wednesday 12 April 2006 09:41, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Wed, 12 Apr 2006, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> > Yes to both. I haven't the faintest idea why they'd block PDFs, but
> > basically everything useful Winders users would want to attach to email
> > (.DOC, .XLS, .ZIP, .JPG) has been used to carry viruses/worms.
> 
>    I have read that all Microsoft document formats have been compromised;
> didn't realize that .jpg files are also in that category. I suppose that
> since nothing Microsoft puts out is immune to being mis-used, the assumption
> is that everyone sends only malware. "Your productivity ... our passion."
> Yeah. Right.
> 

Malware authors need productivity and passion, too.

Windoze is too brain-dead to determine the file type by itself, so it relies 
on extensions. So you'll see weird filenames like foo.jpg.exe. And yes, a lot 
of common file types have been compromised in ways that don't require weirdo 
filenames. Windows happily runs any stray executable, whether or not you want 
it too. Microsoft's feeble efforts to lock it down have succeeded in making 
it even more mysterious to users and admins, but hasn't done a thing to slow 
down its affinity for all the wrong things. It's a form of perversity that 
has almost reached the level of art.

Subject: [PLUG] Interesting Samba problem
Message-ID: <200604121204.32233.ladyfractal@yahoo.com>

Hey folks:

A customer of mine is using a version control product that I support in a 
mixed platform environment.  All of their repositories live on a UNIX file 
system and are mounted by Samba.  The clients are all using development tools 
on Windows machines so when they save their source files to the Samba mount 
they are getting the EOL (^M) character thrown in which makes their build 
scripts complain and then die.  The particular version of the versioning 
software they are using does not have a built-in 'translate EOL' function and 
I was wondering if anyone might know of a hack to make Samba do the heavy 
lifting.  

I initially thought a shell script but that won't work because when the source 
code file is written to the archive library as read-only.  If it can't be 
done, it can't be done and they'll have to continue transferring their source 
code files as binary but I'd like for them to not have to do so.

Thanks in advance.

Cheers
Aj

Aj,

   There is a pair of great linux tools called dos2unix and unix2dos
(Slackware has them as todos and tounix) that translate EOLs from one flavor
to the other. How these can be used in a solution for you is up to folks much
smarter than I am about these things.

From cbrink at brinkrods.com  Wed Apr 12 12:23:12 2006
From: cbrink at brinkrods.com (Christian Brink)
Date: Wed, 12 Apr 2006 12:23:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Interesting Samba problem
In-Reply-To: <200604121204.32233.ladyfractal@yahoo.com>
References: <200604121204.32233.ladyfractal@yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <443D53A0.6090000@brinkrods.com>

Adrienne J Davis wrote:
> Hey folks:
> 
> A customer of mine is using a version control product that I support in a 
> mixed platform environment.  All of their repositories live on a UNIX file 
> system and are mounted by Samba.  The clients are all using development tools 
> on Windows machines so when they save their source files to the Samba mount 
> they are getting the EOL (^M) character thrown in which makes their build 
> scripts complain and then die.  The particular version of the versioning 
> software they are using does not have a built-in 'translate EOL' function and 
> I was wondering if anyone might know of a hack to make Samba do the heavy 
> lifting.  

Back when I did a lot of Samba. I remember the Samba development team 
not being keen on this idea. It's riddled with problems. I would agree 
with them. IIRC there is a hook in the conf - but IMO it's a deal with 
the devil (M$ pun intended).

> I initially thought a shell script but that won't work because when the source 
> code file is written to the archive library as read-only.  If it can't be 
> done, it can't be done and they'll have to continue transferring their source 
> code files as binary but I'd like for them to not have to do so.

I would not recommend saving files as binaries. Binary files are 
versioned as an entire file (AFAIK in most version control systems). So 
you'll end up bloating the repo.

The best solution as I see it, would be if their development environment 
would be able to deal with *nix style line endings and have them switch 
to that.

If that is not an option - other options I can think of:
Switch to a version control system that understands line end translations.
run dos2unix before your 'make' - either find a way to run it on the 
repo or do a check out - conversion - check in

Christian

If the revision system is cvs or svn, I would recommend TortoiseCVS or
TortoiseSVN.

I had very good experiences with TortoiseCVS working in Linux and windows.  Had
to share some of the library and header code between client (Linux)
and server (windows).

Rich:

What I usually do if I have to send a customer/user something like a DOS batch 
file or Bash shell script, both of which my employer filters out, is to 
rename the attachment to .txt or, if a binary, simply remove the extension 
altogther.  I'll then tell the recipient, save the file as type foo and all 
the right things happen.

Cheers
Adrienne

   I have an image (.jpg, .png, .gif ... any flavor that'll work) and I want
to draw a red circle around the existing image. I use the elliptical
selection tool and create the circle (holding the shift key as I drag the
selection). Next, I apply Edit->Stroke selection. That gives me a nice 6 px
width on the circle.

   But, I cannot color that rim. I can fill the circle with a color, but that
obscures the image below. Rather than having the disk filled, I want only the
border filled with color. According to the GIMP help, I'm doing the correct
things, but I'm seeing the expected results.

   Any ideas?

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Apr 13 11:50:15 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)

Subject: [PLUG] Any GIMP Gurus Here? -- PROBLEM RESOLVED
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604131139290.20219@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604131139290.20219@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Thu, 13 Apr 2006, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  But, I cannot color that rim. I can fill the circle with a color, but that
> obscures the image below. Rather than having the disk filled, I want only
> the border filled with color. According to the GIMP help, I'm doing the
> correct things, but I'm seeing the expected results.

   Sigh. As is so often the case, I discovered the solution immediately after
sending the original message. What I had to do was swap the fg and bg colors
before creating the circle. Once I did that, and chose a solid like for the
stroke selection, there it was in red. Ta-da!

Rich

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Apr 13 19:58:28 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

# from Richard C. Steffens
# on Thursday 13 April 2006 07:48 pm:

>Unfortunately, she needs to run a disk space hogging program that will
>only install on the "C" drive (and is only available for Winders).

Sure!  Just login to windows as root and mount C:\\Program\ 
Files\\directory\ name\\here\\ to a partition on the new drive.  Oh, 
wait...  nevermind that.

>Can I boot up the machine with Knoppix (or similar) and copy the first
>drive to the second in a way that, when I swap the drives, the second
>drive boots just as if it was the first drive, but is now 13GB? (I
> would swap cables and adjust jumpers on the drives.)

Sure, but you have to transfer the boot sector or else use the windows 
rescue disk to put a boot sector on the new drive.  IIRC, it is 
somewhat dependent on the drive geometry, so it may be easiest to use 
the startup disk instead of dd.

From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Thu Apr 13 21:34:44 2006
From: danielmyoung at gmail.com (Dan Young)
Date: Thu, 13 Apr 2006 21:34:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0604132134q620e1babw9d6a17d7b36008b8@mail.gmail.com>

On 4/13/06, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> Can I boot up the machine with Knoppix (or similar) and copy the first
> drive to the second in a way that, when I swap the drives, the second
> drive boots just as if it was the first drive, but is now 13GB? (I would
> swap cables and adjust jumpers on the drives.)

Ghost4Linux has bootable ISO images for download. It does either local
or networked disk cloning. A quick peek says if you clone from a
smaller disk to a larger disk, it will leave "some space" (their
words; not very descriptive) unallocated. It uses partimage, according
to the docs, so if Knoppix has that, you might just try it directly.

GNU Parted can resize FAT partitions.

http://sourceforge.net/projects/g4l
http://www.gnu.org/software/parted

Optionally, you could find somebody with Norton/Symantec Ghost, which
can resize partitions during a clone.

Nice thing is, you can afford to screw it up as long as you leave the
original disk alone.

--
Dan Young


From m0gely at telestream.com  Fri Apr 14 00:11:22 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 00:11:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <443F4B1A.6060901@telestream.com>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> My wife has a 4GB hard drive on her Win98 machine and needs more room. 
> The simple solution was to add a 13GB drive as a second drive. 
> Unfortunately, she needs to run a disk space hogging program that will 
> only install on the "C" drive (and is only available for Winders).
> 
> My question:
> 
> Can I boot up the machine with Knoppix (or similar) and copy the first 
> drive to the second in a way that, when I swap the drives, the second 
> drive boots just as if it was the first drive, but is now 13GB? (I would 
> swap cables and adjust jumpers on the drives.)

Yes.  Boot to DOS by pressing F8 just as Windows starts to load, you 
must be quick.  Run FDISK and set the primary partition on the D:\ drive 
to "Active".  Exit FDISK and do "format d: /q /s".  This will quick 
format the drive and make it bootable.  I know on floppies you can just 
do "sys a:" and it will make the disk a system disk (bootable) w/o the 
format, but I can't remember if that works on hard drives.  It's worth a 
try.  If it doesn't work it will just tell you you can't do that.

Now boot to Knoppix and just copy everything over.  Note; I didn't say 
move.  You will get a few files being over written (the boot files), 
that's ok.  The /s option on the format command was still necessary.

Now reboot after swapping your cables and boot to the larger drive to 
verify all went well.

If you're a whiz at partition editing then the dd/image method may be 
fastest.  But this method isn't far behind at all.

-- 
- m0gely
http://quake2.telestream.com/
Q2 | Q3A | Counter-strike

From m0gely at telestream.com  Fri Apr 14 08:02:29 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 08:02:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0604140616510.26244@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0604140616510.26244@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <443FB985.3060205@telestream.com>

Rich Shepard wrote:

>   If you install the larger drive as secondary to the existing one (d:\, I
> believe it would be), could you not copy over most of the applications and
> all of the data from the original drive and leave that for booting and the
> user-unfriendly application that insists on being there and taking up a lot
> of room?
> 
>   Moving applications and all data out of the way should be simpler and
> easier than recreating the existing system on a larger drive. No?

This will most likely cause much grief.  As any shortcuts (icon 
symlinks) will point to the old location.  The entire Start menu in 
userland in Windows is all shortcuts, as well as whatever shortcuts were 
put on the desktop over time.

But that's not the worse part.  The installation of anything more than a 
very simple small program 99% of the time can't just be moved.  Windows 
expects it to be where it was installed at.  The registry or any .ini 
(.conf) files that the program uses to store personal or internal 
configuration info such as paths, would now be wrong as well.

A better approach to this idea would be to use the uninstall utility, 
and remove programs that way.  Applications such as MS Office won't 
delete personal settings, email and such.  It will just remove the 
suite.  Then it could be reinstalled on a different drive and email and 
personalizations will magically return on their own.  Unless you are 
aware of what applications take up the most disk real estate then this 
might be time consuming as well.  Honestly I think my approach is the 
best and easiest, but not for ego.  I do this sort of thing all the time 
so it's easy for me.

-- 
- m0gely
http://quake2.telestream.com/
Q2 | Q3A | Counter-strike

I've personally been less-than-impessed with the LSI hardware, mainly
documentation and linux support... these controllers are present on
the Sun v[2|4]0z Opteron systems, and while they work, it took me
quite a while to find _any_ way to determine what the status of the
array is in linux.

There's some java app that you can run, but is GUI based and not
scriptable. I also found a semi un-maintained C source that polls the
driver and outputs a character device with some info... it's not
complete, and was working on a perl app to run and parse that info for
Nagios.

If you end up with one and can't get it to do what you want, drop me a
line.  I'd suggest finding something that has more out-of-the box
support.

/eli

PS -- Other than that, the Sun v*z series boxen are _really_ great.

Sorry for not doing it in Linux but I have had great success with a program
called BootIT NG.

download the iso.  burn to cd.  boot it.  hit esc to get past the
install screen.
Used the utilities to image from one drive to another.

This was the only program that would correctly move one of my office machines.
Four different versions of Ghost and two different versions of Drive
Image failed.
BootIT NG worked and re-sized the partition in the process.  No real
work on my part.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Apr 14 18:24:57 2006

Subject: [PLUG] Partly Resolved: Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <443F4B1A.6060901@telestream.com>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net> <443F4B1A.6060901@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <44404B69.90709@comcast.net>

m0gely wrote:
> Richard C. Steffens wrote:
>> Can I boot up the machine with Knoppix (or similar) and copy the first 
>> drive to the second in a way that, when I swap the drives, the second 
>> drive boots just as if it was the first drive, but is now 13GB? (I 
>> would swap cables and adjust jumpers on the drives.)
> 
> 
> Yes.  Boot to DOS by pressing F8 just as Windows starts to load, you 
> must be quick.  Run FDISK and set the primary partition on the D:\ drive 
> to "Active".  Exit FDISK and do "format d: /q /s".  This will quick 
> format the drive and make it bootable.  I know on floppies you can just 
> do "sys a:" and it will make the disk a system disk (bootable) w/o the 
> format, but I can't remember if that works on hard drives.  It's worth a 
> try.  If it doesn't work it will just tell you you can't do that.

The 13GB drive used to be a C: drive with Win98 installed. When I put it 
in her machine, I just erased everything, but didn't format it. I ran 
"sys d:" and got no complaints.

> Now boot to Knoppix and just copy everything over.  Note; I didn't say 
> move.  You will get a few files being over written (the boot files), 
> that's ok.  The /s option on the format command was still necessary.

Knoppix reported /dev/hda1 and /dev/hdb1 as mount points. Looking at the 
contents confirmed that hda1 was the C: drive and hdb1 was the D: drive.

I ran:

dd if=/dev/hda1 of=/dev/hdb1 bs=1024k

It completed without complaint.

> Now reboot after swapping your cables and boot to the larger drive to 
> verify all went well.

I left the old 4GB drive disconnected, and put the jumper on the pins to 
indicate the 13GB drive was a primary drive. Then I rebooted and stopped 
by BIOS and ran auto detect on the hard drive.

The machine booted just fine and it looks like everything is there. But, 
  the properties report shows the drive as only 4.02 GB. I rebooted in 
MS-DOS mode and ran fdisk to have it display the partition info. It 
thinks the drive is 13GB.

> If you're a whiz at partition editing then the dd/image method may be 
> fastest.  But this method isn't far behind at all.

I re-booted Knoppix, and after a bit of searching on the web, discovered 
that Knoppix 3.2 (that's the CD I have), doesn't have parted. On the 
other hand, it does have qtparted. Sigh. Anyway, I ran that and had a 
look at hda. It shows it to be a 13GB partition, too.

So, while the machine will work for her tomorrow, I'd still like to find 
a way to make Winders realize that there's another 9GB out there it can use!

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From dave at dhdo.org  Fri Apr 14 18:57:21 2006
From: dave at dhdo.org (Dave)
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 18:57:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partly Resolved: Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <44404B69.90709@comcast.net>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net> <443F4B1A.6060901@telestream.com>
	<44404B69.90709@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200604141857.21643.dave@dhdo.org>

dd is a little lower-level than I think you probably wanted.  It will make a 
perfect image (assuming no errors) of the drive's sectors -- including things 
like the partition table.  

It's why dd is great for making CD and floppy disk images, and for copying 
from one identical drive to another.. but not so much for transferring files.

The best solution, honestly, would be, I think, ghost followed by Partition 
Magic, because they're better at dealing with Microsoft's filesystems.

On Friday 14 April 2006 18:24, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> I ran:
>
> dd if=/dev/hda1 of=/dev/hdb1 bs=1024k
>
> It completed without complaint.


From denish at dslnorthwest.net  Fri Apr 14 18:57:45 2006
From: denish at dslnorthwest.net (Denis Heidtmann)
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 18:57:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] From CCD World--Linux-based systems
Message-ID: <44405319.6030603@dslnorthwest.net>

Any scientific astronomers here?

-------- Original Message --------
Subject: CCD-world: The Amateur Sky Survey
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 16:03:17 -0500
From: Thomas F. Droege <droege at fastmail.fm>
Reply-To: Optical & SWIR imager development for professional astronomy 
<ccd-world at ctio.noao.edu>
To: ccd-world at ctio.noao.edu

The Amateur Sky Survey (tass) is closing it's doors.

The reason for posting here is that some of the equipment might be
useful to the scientific community.

The idea of tass was to build camera systems suitable for an all sky
survey and give them away (without software) to programmers found on
the internet. I call this the "Field of Dreams" scheme.  If you
build the systems, the software will come.  The scheme actually
worked. Four dual camera systems were given away and two of the
recipients wrote the code needed. Three dual systems were operated at
Batavia, IL and have taken about 230 million measurements over three
years of stars in the northern sky. Each consists of simultaneous
measurements in V and I in the range of 7 to 15.

The systems consist of two 2k x 2k CCD442A detectors and 100
mm dia f/4 optics on a simple polar mount.  The optics are narrow
band to match the filter used.  This allowed an improved flat field
for the 5 element optics.

The best description is at:

http://www.tass-survey.org/tass/showtell/st0010.html

More information than you can stand at:

http://www.tass-survey.org/tass/tass.shtml

The electronics is home designed and works just fine.  The best I have
done for noise is 10 e- or so but I only cool to -20 C.  No
electronic reason not to get to 3 e- with proper cooling.  The
electronics is 2 channel and there is some cross talk 1/1000 or so.
Not critical for me since I am sky noise limited.  One could always
run single channel systems.  There is lots of spare electronics.

There is lots of stuff available here in Batavia, IL that will go
into the ash can with my impending demise.  I have cancer and it has
gotten to the state that I can no longer scramble up on the roof to
run the telescopes.  There are pretty good drawings for all the parts
and good electronics drawings.

Some of the items:
16 completed and tested 2k x 2k CCD442A cameras.  TEC cooled
each camera has a built in V or I filter.  There are a few spare
  2" x 2" filters.
2 Unused CCD442As lumigen coated, taped windows
16 sets of dual channel electronics.
5 completed dual channel systems
32 lenses various numbers of B, V, I, and R
Of the 20 CCD442A CCDs, most are class 3 but the company was
generous and they are probably better than class 3.  They look very
good to me.
Many computers running on linux.  System requires an ISA slot so I
have a big collection of recent ISA slot computers that run linux.

Contact Tom Droege  droege at fastmail.fm

I will accumulate requests and then give stuff away for the best
science use.

From dave at dhdo.org  Fri Apr 14 22:57:32 2006
From: dave at dhdo.org (Dave)
Date: Fri, 14 Apr 2006 22:57:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partly Resolved: Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <444082E6.8060904@comcast.net>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net> <200604141857.21643.dave@dhdo.org>
	<444082E6.8060904@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200604142257.34878.dave@dhdo.org>

On Friday 14 April 2006 22:21, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Dave wrote:
> > dd is a little lower-level than I think you probably wanted.  It will
> > make a perfect image (assuming no errors) of the drive's sectors --
> > including things like the partition table.
> >
> > It's why dd is great for making CD and floppy disk images, and for
> > copying from one identical drive to another.. but not so much for
> > transferring files.
> >
> > The best solution, honestly, would be, I think, ghost followed by
> > Partition Magic, because they're better at dealing with Microsoft's
> > filesystems.
>
> My wife has tested a few programs, and they seem to work correctly.
> She'll give it more of a workout tomorrow. Assuming all that works, then
> it would appear the dd did the same thing that Ghost would have done.
> Does Partition Magic change anything in the registry, or something else,
> that would make the OS recognize what the partition table sees as the
> drive size? Is there some kind of end-of-partition marker that needs to
> move?

The partition table for drives is stored in the first few sectors of the 
drive, so when you dd the drive, you end up with the partition table of the 
old drive (and all it pointed to, including the size) in the new drive's 
image.  You might be able to get away, if you have the new drive already in 
place and "working", just installing Partition magic and resizing the 
drive -- the BIOS seems to recognize the real size of the drive, so it 
shouldn't be a big deal... (shouldn't)

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Sat Apr 15 13:01:01 2006
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 15 Apr 2006 13:01:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <444140FA.5040907@comcast.net>
References: <443F0D84.6060904@comcast.net>	<ca5165530604140834q62e289f5u42848898a8fb329b@mail.gmail.com>	<44404EC0.704@comcast.net>	<444120CF.9020601@telestream.com>
	<444140FA.5040907@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <444150FD.90803@axpr.net>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> To start over:
> 
> 1. Delete all the stuff I put on the large drive with dd.
> 
> 2. Boot up with Knoppix and proceed with the copy as described, above.
> 
> Right?

Okay... the problem here is you copied over an image of your 4GB hard 
disk on to your 13GB hard disk.  That image contains some magic at the 
start of it that says "I have a single 4GB partition and that is all" so 
your 13GB disk can't be fully realized.  What needs to happen is that 
magic at the beginning of the disk needs to say "I have a 13GB 
partition" and then the filesystem that lives inside that partition 
(FAT32) needs to be fixed to take up the remainder of the single drive 
partition.

Now, you can either resize the existing 4GB partition using some magic 
like parted (which is able to resize partitions) OR you can start from 
scratch.  Repartition the 13GB drive in to a single 13GB partition (with 
fdisk or similar), create a FAT32 partition on it (with mkfs.msdos or 
similar), put the windows boot magic on it (with a DOS floppy and the 
"SYS" command), then *copy* over the *files* from the old disk.  Use cp. 
  Not dd.

Note the format/boot magic steps can be combined with a DOS floppy as 
mentioned by somebody else before using the /s flag in DOS's FORMAT command.

Rob

From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Apr 15 16:13:14 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 15 Apr 2006 16:13:14 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Partly Resolved: Copying Hard Disk to Hard Disk
In-Reply-To: <20060415164142.31641464F@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200604152313.k3FNDEO9071704@m5p.com>

>From: "Richard C. Steffens" <rsteff at comcast.net>
> Knoppix reported /dev/hda1 and /dev/hdb1 as mount points. Looking at the 
> contents confirmed that hda1 was the C: drive and hdb1 was the D: drive.
> 
> I ran:
> 
> dd if=/dev/hda1 of=/dev/hdb1 bs=1024k
> 
> It completed without complaint.

> The machine booted just fine and it looks like everything is there. But, 
>   the properties report shows the drive as only 4.02 GB. I rebooted in 
> MS-DOS mode and ran fdisk to have it display the partition info. It 
> thinks the drive is 13GB.

What you need is a filesystem resizer for MSDOS. MSDOS is reporting the
amount of space on the _filesystem_, not the size of the slice. You made
an exact image of the filesystem, including the filesystem structure. The
trick is that structure includes the size.

There are three approaches. First, you can change the size of the slice
to be 4.02GB, and then make a second slice (partition) that would appear
as D: (then use `format D:` to make the filesystem).

Second, you can use a filesystem resizer to enlarge the filesystem. The
one that comes to mind is FIPS, though that was mainly used to shrink
FAT filesystems in the bad olde days. I also haven't seen much mention
of it recently, so I've no idea whether it has been maintained.

Third, you can go back, and create a new filesystem on the disk and
simply copy file by file.
